201501	1940	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	The greater-than-expected strength in second- half spending was concentrated in personal consumption expenditures, and as you may recall, most of that good news was already in hand by the time of the December FOMC meeting.	11	1
201501	1941	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	This morning we received the advance report on durable goods orders and shipments in December.	11	1
201501	1942	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	This release was just a shade weaker than we’d been looking for, but the broad contour of our near-term outlook remains very much intact.	11	1

201501	1944	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	The level of total payrolls in December was about 50,000 higher than we had expected, and the unemployment rate—shown in panel 2—was 5.6 percent last month, 0.1 percentage point below our December forecast.	11	1
201501	1945	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	Panel 3 shows our labor market conditions index, or LMCI, which attempts to extract a common signal from 19 indicators of labor market conditions.	11	1
201501	1946	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	In the inset box, the red bars show the monthly change in the LMCI based on the information available at the time of the December Tealbook, while the gray bars show the latest estimates.	11	1
201501	1947	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	Viewed through this lens, labor market conditions have improved noticeably during the past six weeks.	11	1
201501	1948	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	A key question for your deliberation now, as always, is where the real side of the economy stands relative to its sustainable position.	11	1
201501	1949	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	Panel 4 provides three perspectives on that issue.	11	1
201501	1950	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	The dotted blue line shows the traditional Tealbook method for assessing this question—namely, the percentage difference between actual and potential GDP.	11	1
201501	1951	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	The red line shows the “production function” version of the output gap as estimated in EDO, one of the DSGE models that we maintain at the Board.	11	1
201501	1952	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	The black line—labeled FRB/US—provides an early-stage report on a different approach, one that we aim to build on during the next couple of years.	11	1
201501	1953	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	This measure is based on work done by Charles Fleischman and John Roberts.	11	1
201501	1954	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	It has the virtue of attempting to combine the information from a variety of production, income, labor market, and price indicators while also explicitly allowing for measurement error.	11	1
201501	1955	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	According to all three measures, considerable progress has been made in closing the gap in resource utilization in recent years.	11	1
201501	1956	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	Indeed, by any reasonable standard of precision, the economy now is operating at or near its sustainable position.	11	1
201501	1957	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	I would note that the confidence interval shown around the FRB/US estimate ignores both coefficient uncertainty and model uncertainty, and so it surely understates the true level of imprecision.	11	1
201501	1958	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	I look forward to updating you on our progress as we pursue an ambitious line of work to refine this measure and put it more at the center of our macroanalysis.	11	1
201501	1959	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	Turning from the “nowcast” to the forecast for real activity, three main considerations are relevant.	11	1
201501	1960	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	First, the greater-than-expected strength in spending and the labor market during the second half of last year implies some greater momentum in real activity coming into the first half of this year than we previously believed.	11	1
201501	1961	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	Second, the stronger exchange value of the dollar that Steve discussed implies some restraint on spending and employment.	11	1
201501	1962	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	By the end of 2017, the stronger dollar relative to the path in the December Tealbook would subtract 0.4 percent from the level of real GDP and would, taken alone, add about 0.2 percentage point to the unemployment rate, all else being equal.	11	1
201501	1963	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	Third, the further plunge in oil prices that Steve also mentioned provides, on balance, a countervailing boost to aggregate demand.	11	1
201501	1964	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	To be sure, not all of the effects of the decline in oil prices on the US economy will be positive.	11	1
201501	1965	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	In particular, we anticipate some considerable reductions in drilling and mining investment in the next few quarters.	11	1
201501	1966	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	As well, if oil prices stay low, oil production should level off this year and then edge lower, in contrast to the steep uptrend of the past few years.	11	1
201501	1967	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	And, as suggested by work, authored jointly by our Federal Reserve Bank of Dallas colleague Mine Yücel, states, large fossil fuel sectors will take some sizable hits to employment relative to what they would have experienced otherwise.	11	1
201501	1968	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	All that said, we continue to see the drop in oil prices as a plus for the US economy, but the question remains as to how much of a plus.	11	1
201501	1969	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	During the intermeeting period, we spent some time digging into this question.	11	1
201501	1970	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	As Hess Chung summarized in his briefing to the Board yesterday, there simply is no way to know with any precision.	11	1
201501	1971	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	Absent any add-factoring, the FRB/US and SIGMA models both predict that a persistent decline in oil prices similar to what we’ve seen since the middle of last year should boost the level of real GDP by roughly 0.4 percent after two years.	11	1
201501	1972	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	However, the median estimate reported in an internal BIS review implies a boost to the level of GDP of 0.7 percent, and a VAR model that Board staff maintain implies an output dividend of 1 percent.	11	1
201501	1973	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	But the range of uncertainty surrounding all of these estimates is wide.	11	1
201501	1974	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	To illustrate, I would simply note that the estimates surveyed by the BIS ranged from 0.3 to 1.5 percent.	11	1
201501	1975	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	Moreover, last week’s staff memo to the FOMC discussed a number of relevant risks, including ones coming from the financial side of the economy.	11	1
201501	1976	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	On balance, though, we judged our previous FRB/US-based rule of thumb to have been a little on the slim side, and so, in preparing our latest forecast, we adopted an elasticity in line with the median from the BIS review.	11	1
201501	1977	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	Even so, the further drop in oil prices since the December Tealbook adds only about 0.3 percent to the level of real GDP in the baseline forecast by the end of 2017.	11	1
201501	1978	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	On net, the three factors that I’ve cited—stronger incoming data, a stronger exchange value of the dollar, and lower oil prices—were roughly a wash, leaving our medium-term projection for GDP growth little changed from December.	11	1
201501	1979	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	Or, putting the point somewhat differently, the upward revision to the level of activity in the near term carries forward through much of the projection period, yielding a slightly higher degree of resource utilization in this forecast than in the previous one.	11	1
201501	1980	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	One other important endeavor during the intermeeting period was to provide you with another installment in our series of memos laying out various aspects of our forecast methodology—this installment covering the methods we use to set the main financial variables in the projection.	11	1
201501	1981	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	The last two panels of your exhibit summarize the inflation outlook.	11	1
201501	1982	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	As shown in panel 5, the near-term outlook for headline PCE inflation now looks much more negative than before, mainly on account of the further plunge in energy prices.	11	1

201501	1986	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	As in previous Tealbooks, we expect core inflation to increase slowly over the forecast period, reaching 1.6 percent next year and 1.8 percent in 2017.	11	1

201501	1989	2015-01-28	meeting	WILCOX	For now, we continue to judge a “steady as she goes” inflation forecast as the best base case for your deliberations, but we perceive the odds of a shortfall as having risen.	11	1
201501	1990	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	I’ll be referring to the handout labeled “Financial Stability Developments.”	11	1
201501	1991	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	My remarks draw on our recent QS financial stability report.	11	1
201501	1992	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	Focusing first on our topline assessment, we continue to judge overall vulnerabilities in the financial system as moderate, as highlighted in the box at the top left of your first exhibit.	11	1
201501	1993	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	This assessment reflects the interaction of three key factors.	11	1
201501	1994	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	Specifically, while we judge valuation pressures, on balance, across a range of markets, to be notable, the degree of leverage and maturity transformation in the financial system is low to moderate, and the level of borrowing by the nonfinancial sector has not picked up appreciably.	11	1

201501	1998	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	Our review of valuations across a range of markets highlighted continued or building valuation pressures in corporate debt markets and the commercial real estate area.	11	1

201501	2001	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	More accommodative underwriting in this market is apparent in rising loan-to-value ratios and increases in interest-only loans in CMBS pools, as well as in the continued easing in lending standards on commercial real estate loans reported in the Senior Loan Officer Opinion Survey on Bank Lending Practices.	11	1
201501	2002	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	The remaining exhibits focus on corporate debt markets.	11	1

201501	2004	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	Yields on investment-grade bonds, the green line, are little changed, on net, since the October assessment.	11	1
201501	2005	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	That said, the yield on the 10-year Treasury is down notably over the same period, so spreads on both high-yield and investment-grade bonds have widened, on net, over the past several months.	11	1
201501	2006	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	This widening appears to reflect both some increased concerns about near-term credit risk and some reduction in risk appetite.	11	1
201501	2007	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	A contributor to the increase in high-yield spreads is pessimism about the outlook for credit quality in the energy sector: As indicated by the teal line, yields on high-yield bonds of the energy sector have jumped up.	11	1
201501	2008	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	As discussed in a box in our report, we do not anticipate significant strains on the broader financial system due to the drop in oil prices because leveraged positions related to oil appear limited and the degree of loss-absorbing capital at large banks is much increased.	11	1
201501	2009	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	Still, some regional and small banks with concentrated exposures could face notable strains related to loans that are likely to default at the current level of the price of oil.	11	1
201501	2010	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	The widening in spreads in the riskier segment of the corporate debt market has been accompanied by a slowing in issuance, as shown in the bottom-left panel.	11	1
201501	2011	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	The combination of junk bond and leveraged loan issuance slowed in the fourth quarter, largely reflecting a slowing in leveraged loans due to reduced refinancing activity as financial conditions tightened.	11	1
201501	2012	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	The level of outstanding debt in these segments (the black line) has advanced rapidly over the past few years, and it remains to be seen whether recent developments represent a sustained softening in growth.	11	1
201501	2013	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	We have received hints that underwriting quality for leveraged loans may have improved late last year.	11	1
201501	2014	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	Average debt multiples (the bottom-right panel), which are one measure of underwriting quality, declined slightly in the fourth quarter for large LBO loans and middle-market loans, perhaps reflecting some response to the intensified supervisory message following the 2013 leveraged lending guidance.	11	1
201501	2015	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	Possibly also in response to the guidance, there have been indications that underwriting of deals by smaller broker-dealers or private equity firms has picked up.	11	1
201501	2016	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	Your second exhibit looks at broad measures of leverage and maturity transformation.	11	1
201501	2017	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	The rapid growth in riskier corporate debt has boosted net leverage among speculative-grade corporations (the black line in the upper-left chart).	11	1
201501	2018	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	The debt burden, as measured by the interest expense ratio (the red line), has stayed low, but rapid growth of the type seen in recent years has, in the past, presaged elevated defaults in subsequent years.	11	1
201501	2019	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	In the aggregate, business debt relative to nominal GDP (the lower-shaded portion in the exhibit on the right) has increased only modestly, while household credit relative to nominal GDP has moved sideways through the third quarter.	11	1
201501	2020	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	We have yet to see a detectable turn in the credit cycle, and the reduction in private nonfinancial debt relative to overall income seen over the past five years is an important contributor to our assessment that overall financial stability risks are moderate.	11	1
201501	2021	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	Nonetheless, we are attuned to the fact that private nonfinancial debt relative to GDP remains above levels seen prior to the mid-2000s.	11	1
201501	2022	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	A range of indicators suggests that the degree of maturity transformation and liquidity risks in the financial sector remain moderate.	11	1
201501	2023	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	For example, the ratio of net short-term wholesale funding of the broad financial sector to nominal GDP, shown in the middle-left panel, remains subdued.	11	1
201501	2024	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	We also have seen a sizable increase in the liquidity buffers at large banks, presumably reflecting the phase-in of the Basel III liquidity-coverage ratio.	11	1
201501	2025	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	Nonetheless, we highlighted in our assessment the possibility that the large increase in the role of bond and loan mutual funds promising daily liquidity when the liquidity of the assets held by such funds is uncertain could produce strains if flows out of such funds were to surge.	11	1
201501	2026	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	Leverage in the financial sector also appears low.	11	1
201501	2027	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	Much of this assessment stems from the increase in loss-absorbing capital within the banking sector:  As reported in the middle-right panel, the leverage ratio (the blue line) has continued to edge up at CCAR banks, while their Tier 1 ratio (the black line) has remained at a level much above pre-crisis levels, reflecting the combination of Basel III requirements and the resilience required in the annual stress test exercises.	11	1
201501	2028	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	The current DFAST and CCAR exercises are under way and will be concluded in early March.	11	1
201501	2029	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	Outside the banking sector, pockets of the financial system continue to use high degrees of leverage to boost returns.	11	1
201501	2030	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	Hedge fund leverage, shown at the bottom left, remains near post-crisis highs.	11	1
201501	2031	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	Recently, several hedge funds active in foreign exchange trades made headlines by reporting significant losses in relation to the sudden appreciation of the Swiss franc.	11	1
201501	2032	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	As highlighted in the first bullet to the right, we expect the effects on the financial system of the sharp appreciation of the Swiss franc earlier this month to be limited.	11	1
201501	2033	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	At the moment, no significant counterparty issues have emerged.	11	1
201501	2034	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	Some large US banks experienced trading losses, but these appear modest.	11	1
201501	2035	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	As discussed in the second bullet, one source of volatility since October has been a rekindling of concerns related to Greece and peripheral euro-zone economies, and the potential for surprises remains palpable.	11	1
201501	2036	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	Direct exposures of the US financial system to proximate flashpoints appear notably lower than those seen two to three years ago.	11	1
201501	2037	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	We also continue to investigate, as highlighted in the final bullet, the factors that contributed to the volatility in the Treasury market on October 15.	11	1
201501	2038	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	The staff has been coordinating with the CFTC to share data and analyses, but work remains ongoing.	11	1
201501	2039	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	We have also included, on the last page of your handout, our overall stability dashboard for your reference.	11	1
201501	2040	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	This dashboard summarizes our assessment and provides a comparison of conditions today with our current assessment of conditions in 2004 and 2007.	11	1

201501	2178	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	So, for example, if something historically looks very low right now, but very low is still a terrible position, our quantitative folks would say, “Oh, that’s fine, but structurally there may be a problem.”	12	1
201501	2179	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	That supplemental report looks at many of the same factors that we looked at here.	12	1
201501	2180	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	Our judgmental report looks at many others, but it faces the same set of challenges.	12	1
201501	2181	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	We try to say, “How do conditions look today relative to history?”	12	1
201501	2182	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	And that’s easy, because in the case of those indicators for which we have some history, we can say, “Well, things are high or low, and high is good or low is bad,” and put together an assessment.	12	1
201501	2183	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	The more difficult challenge is that, historically, there may be indicators—for example, like the historical amount of leverage of banking organizations—for which our assessment now is that it always was too low.	12	1
201501	2184	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	And so, of course, it’s high now, but the question is:  Is it high enough?	12	1
201501	2185	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	If you look at the quantitative indicators, various categories are not green or yellow as they are in our judgmental assessment, but are ice-blue cold.	12	1
201501	2186	2015-01-28	meeting	KILEY	So, for example, if you look at the picture for capitalization in the bank sector, all of the numbers are much higher than before, much higher than at any other time for the data that we have.	12	1

201501	2208	2015-01-28	meeting	LAUBACH	Taylor (1999) would say it already would be at 2 percent now.	12	1

201501	2211	2015-01-28	meeting	LAUBACH	I think the peculiar thing is in that scenario, even though inflation is so low from a real-activity perspective, the economy looks reasonably healthy because the unemployment rate is running relatively low.	12	1
201501	2212	2015-01-28	meeting	LAUBACH	So the rule looks at it as though the economy is actually strong.	12	1

201501	2363	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	First, with regard to my District—and I remind you, 98 percent of the output of my District is generated by one state, the state of Texas—labor market data released on Friday were in keeping with what we at the Dallas Fed had forecast and I had reported to this Committee over the past several months.	13	1
201501	2364	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	Despite the precipitous declines in oil prices since June, and particularly since November, payroll activity in Texas increased at a 3.67 percent annual pace in December, spot-on to our forecast of 3.6 percent.	13	1
201501	2365	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	Employment growth did fall by at least 1 percentage point in the energy-intensive areas of Houston, Midland, and Odessa, but expanded in the technology-intensive areas of Dallas, Arlington, Fort Worth, and places like College Station and Austin.	13	1
201501	2366	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	And, thus, the state unemployment rate fell 0.3 percentage point in December to 4.6 percent.	13	1
201501	2367	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	There is no question that sustained lower energy prices affect Texas.	13	1
201501	2368	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	We produce 37 percent of the nation’s crude oil and one-fourth of its natural gas.	13	1
201501	2369	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	That said, and I think this is the tendency of J.P.	13	1
201501	2370	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	Morgan and others who have looked at us, this is not the 1980s all over again.	13	1
201501	2371	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	First, our economy is far better diversified than it was.	13	1
201501	2372	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	Oil and gas and mining and all of the support services for those three sectors account for 2.6 percent of total nonfarm employment in Texas.	13	1
201501	2373	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	The share of oil and gas in Texas’s nominal GDP or the state equivalent is 13 percent.	13	1
201501	2374	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	Those shares, however, are well below their peaks in the early 1980s, when oil and gas accounted for 5 percent of employment, almost one-fifth of our output, and 17.7 percent of Texas state government revenues.	13	1
201501	2375	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	Today, because of a diversified economy and a diversified tax base, oil and gas accounts, as of the end of 2014, were only 4.1 percent of the total state revenue.	13	1
201501	2376	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	Second, thus far, the oil price decline is less than that experienced in the 1986 crash, although it may approach the same.	13	1
201501	2377	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	Prices fell 63 percent in that period, from November 1985 to July 1986.	13	1
201501	2378	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	And from June 2014 to now, prices have declined 53 percent, although I used the number 55 percent in the presentation earlier.	13	1
201501	2379	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	Third, simultaneous with the oil price debacle of the mid-1980s, we did have the US Tax Reform Act of 1986, which changed how commercial real estate was taxed.	13	1
201501	2380	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	It crimped CRE profitability and compounded the severe downturn in commercial real estate in Texas and the banks that loaned to commercial real estate investors.	13	1
201501	2381	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	And, while we do have some small banks in the panhandle, in the Permian Basin, where activity is related to oil and gas indirectly—and almost everything they do is related to oil and gas—most of the middle to larger-sized banks we have contacted say their energy customers are hedged, and that they were hedged before they made their E&P loans to them.	13	1
201501	2382	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	I also note that large banks and other investors generally do not have highly leveraged positions related to oil.	13	1
201501	2383	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	Across the LISCC and community banks, outstanding oil and gas loans are, at most, 15 percent or, at the highest that we can see, 40 percent of Tier 1 capital.	13	1
201501	2384	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	Fourth, it’s important to note that in the mid-1980s, the oil futures trading market was in its nascent stages.	13	1
201501	2385	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	It was not very deep, like it is today.	13	1
201501	2386	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	Of course, before 1982, basically there was a fix for oil prices kind of like the gold market spot fix.	13	1
201501	2387	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	But if you look at charts of spot WTI, as we have done, or, most importantly, refined petroleum products going back to 1954, you will notice that before 1986, there was very little short-term volatility, whereas post-1986, as trading markets developed, there are significant price movements in both directions.	13	1
201501	2388	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	That shift in volatility has had an effect on the way bankers and Texas investors finance projects, the success of which depends on the price of oil and gas.	13	1
201501	2389	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	Now, having said all of that, we do expect that if prices remain where they are for a prolonged period—remember, I have argued, based on my contacts, that we believe that we are in a price-discovery process led by the Saudis, the Kuwaitis, and the Emirati.	13	1
201501	2390	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	If this were to last more than a year or even two, we at the Federal Reserve Bank of Dallas expect that, on net, the growth of nonfarm employment will be crimped by job losses in this sector and in related sectors.	13	1
201501	2391	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	And the widely quoted model by two of our economists, Mine Yücel and Michael Plante, indicates a loss of 140,000 or so jobs both directly in this sector and due to knock-on effects.	13	1
201501	2392	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	Netting out the benefits to consumers and business users of energy, the bottom line for my District, for Texas, is that we presently expect job growth of 2 to 2½ percent in 2015.	13	1
201501	2393	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	Being half-Norwegian, I’ll say that this is not Edvard Munch’s portrait of The Scream as JP Morgan and others have forecast.	13	1
201501	2394	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	We are, however, going to see a slowdown in our employment growth rate, but to the pace of the rest of the United States.	13	1
201501	2395	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	So we will have weaker but still positive employment growth.	13	1
201501	2396	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	Let me just add that we have released our manufacturing index on Monday.	13	1

201501	2399	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	It’s half of what it was at its peak in 1982, when it was 0.8 percent.	13	1
201501	2400	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	The nominal share of the oil and gas sector in US GDP is 2.2 percent.	13	1

201501	2404	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	So we concur at the Dallas Reserve Bank with the summary of the Board memo on oil prices in the Tealbook forecast.	13	1
201501	2405	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	For the United States as a whole, supply- induced lower oil prices are a net benefit to the US economy, and I would say, as I said to you on the phone, Madam Chair, especially for the two lowest-income quartiles.	13	1
201501	2406	2015-01-28	meeting	FISHER	With regard to the effects of oil and gas price declines on inflation, lower prices temporarily reduce headline inflation but do not, we believe, have a sustained appreciable effect on core inflation—of course, depending on longer-term inflation expectations being well anchored and on an indication that we at the central bank do not indicate that we are responding directly to movements in retail fuel prices.	13	1

201501	2454	2015-01-28	meeting	LACKER	Falling gasoline prices have no doubt provided a significant boost to real income and real spending, but this is a one-time level effect, and the growth effect is likely to dissipate in a couple of quarters.	13	1

201501	2463	2015-01-28	meeting	LACKER	Turning to inflation, I’d like to thank the staff for their extensive and very timely analysis of inflation expectations.	13	1
201501	2464	2015-01-28	meeting	LACKER	The TIPS-based measures of inflation compensation have been grinding lower over the past several months, and it’s natural to worry about whether that reflects an erosion in credibility of our commitment to 2 percent inflation.	13	1
201501	2465	2015-01-28	meeting	LACKER	It’s striking that, as documented in the Tealbook and the accompanying memo, an array of approaches using different methods and different data all indicate that longer-run inflation expectations—as opposed to compensation—have been quite stable, even as headline and core inflation have fallen in recent months.	13	1
201501	2466	2015-01-28	meeting	LACKER	If oil prices were to decline further, it would put further downward pressure on headline inflation and might yet pose a risk to inflation expectations.	13	1
201501	2467	2015-01-28	meeting	LACKER	However, if oil prices have reached bottom for this cycle, as it appears may be the case, then the stability thus far of measures of expected inflation would make me fairly confident that we will emerge with inflation expectations well anchored near our target, and that core inflation will move back to 2 percent.	13	1
201501	2468	2015-01-28	meeting	LACKER	Barring further shocks, I would expect that to occur somewhat more rapidly than at the glacial pace shown in the Tealbook.	13	1
201501	2469	2015-01-28	meeting	LACKER	The striking divergence between inflation compensation and inflation expectations over the past few months highlights the importance of the distinction between the two.	13	1
201501	2470	2015-01-28	meeting	LACKER	At times it’s easy to treat compensation and expectations as synonymous, but I’m afraid that we are not always careful enough about this difference in our external communications.	13	1
201501	2471	2015-01-28	meeting	LACKER	We have been careful with the literal wording of our statement.	13	1

201501	2473	2015-01-28	meeting	LACKER	That’s the sentence in the first paragraph.	13	1
201501	2474	2015-01-28	meeting	LACKER	But this may place too much emphasis on measures that we know are flawed as indicators of expected inflation.	13	1
201501	2475	2015-01-28	meeting	LACKER	It leaves out the fact that market-based measures of inflation expectations have also remained stable.	13	1
201501	2476	2015-01-28	meeting	LACKER	I think we should be quite mindful, as we go forward, of that distinction between inflation expectations and compensation, and in future communications I think we should try to find a way to deemphasize inflation compensation and draw more attention to market-based measures of expected inflation.	13	1

201501	2503	2015-01-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Of course, the drop in energy prices has pushed down headline year-over-year inflation numbers to zero and even into negative territory.	13	1
201501	2504	2015-01-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Even for core inflation, I expect the downward price pressures from a stronger dollar and the path to a lower energy price will offset for a time the upward pressure arising from reduced slack.	13	1
201501	2505	2015-01-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Based on the experience of the past two decades, these transitory influences should not have persistently depressing effects on inflation, and I continue to expect a gradual move back of inflation toward our 2 percent objective over the next two years.	13	1
201501	2506	2015-01-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	I find some support for this view when I look at disaggregated PCE price data.	13	1
201501	2507	2015-01-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	First, and I actually have this written down, the trimmed mean PCE price index—which I do refer to quite often here and in the public, because I do find it a useful measure of underlying inflation—shows little sign of deceleration.	13	1
201501	2508	2015-01-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Second, my staff finds that, at least so far, the disinflationary pressures seen in overall PCE prices are driven by a relatively narrow set of items.	13	1
201501	2509	2015-01-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	That is, the current downward price movements are not nearly as broad based as they were, for example, during the 2009 to 2010 episode, when expenditure-weighted diffusion indexes showed the price declines distributed across a wide swath of categories.	13	1
201501	2510	2015-01-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	A key driver of my expectation that inflation will gradually return to our objective is the reduction in economic slack, but there is some uncertainty about the strength of this linkage, and a Phillips curve, broadly interpreted, can be formulated in many different ways.	13	1
201501	2511	2015-01-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	In this regard, I too appreciated the Tealbook’s special “Alternative View” box that examined a nonlinear version of the Phillips curve as a substitute for the Tealbook’s baseline specification.	13	1
201501	2512	2015-01-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	More generally, I think such alternative perspectives are really valuable and helpful, and I applaud each and every one of them for helping us avoid the trap of groupthink and really helping us think through some of these important issues.	13	1
201501	2513	2015-01-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Now, theoretical support for a nonlinear Phillips curve can be found in the downward rigidity of nominal wages.	13	1
201501	2514	2015-01-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	As the Tealbook showed, the problem faced by standard Phillips curve models is that, despite the substantial fall in unemployment over the past several years, nominal compensation growth has remained tepid.	13	1
201501	2515	2015-01-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	As I described last April, my staff has found that this pattern of weak nominal wage growth during the early and middle parts of the economic recovery is not unique to this episode.	13	1
201501	2516	2015-01-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	In fact, in the recoveries of the early 1990s and the early 2000s, we saw a very similar pattern in which wage gains lagged the cycle.	13	1
201501	2517	2015-01-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	In each episode, unemployment gaps narrowed significantly before nominal wage growth picked up from low levels.	13	1
201501	2518	2015-01-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	To explain this lag in wage acceleration, my staff focused on difficulties in cutting nominal wages.	13	1
201501	2519	2015-01-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	The constraint on wage cuts was particularly binding after the Great Recession, due to the severity of economic shocks along with the low inflation rate.	13	1
201501	2520	2015-01-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	As a result, a very high share of workers reported that they earned the same wage as they had a year earlier.	13	1
201501	2521	2015-01-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Now, in a flexible market, many of these workers would receive wage cuts during the recession.	13	1
201501	2522	2015-01-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	So wage growth fell, in fact, by less during the recession than it would have otherwise.	13	1
201501	2523	2015-01-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	As a result, once the economy starts to recover, there is a stock of past-deferred wage cuts that need to be slowly overcome as inflation and productivity growth bring real wages back into the balance before nominal wage growth can return to normal.	13	1
201501	2524	2015-01-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Additional recent work by my staff finds further support for this mechanism by looking at cross-industry comparisons.	13	1
201501	2525	2015-01-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	They show that this stock of pent-up wage cuts that needs to be worked off is bigger in those industries for which the downward nominal wage rigidity appears to be most prevalent.	13	1
201501	2526	2015-01-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	These are the industries in which nominal wage growth has been slow relative to declines in unemployment.	13	1
201501	2527	2015-01-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	In industries for which rigidities are less widespread, nominal wage growth has returned to levels more consistent with the current labor market conditions.	13	1
201501	2528	2015-01-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	The key takeaway from all of this work is that the recent tepid wage growth is, in fact, entirely consistent with an improving labor market in a low-inflation environment.	13	1
201501	2529	2015-01-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	As we close in on full employment over the next year, the return to more normal patterns of wage growth should reemerge.	13	1
201501	2530	2015-01-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Indeed, we are seeing incipient signs of this normalization already with the share of workers experiencing zero wage adjustments starting to come down.	13	1
201501	2531	2015-01-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Now, all told, this process should help inflation move back to target.	13	1
201501	2532	2015-01-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Thank you.	13	1
201501	2533	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	May I ask a question?	13	1
201501	2534	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	Yes.	13	1
201501	2535	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	One of the things that I thought was that unemployment is usually the lagging indicator, and it seems to be well ahead of wages and prices at the moment.	13	1
201501	2536	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	Is that unusual?	13	1
201501	2537	2015-01-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	I think my view is that unemployment is a lagging indicator relative to, say, GDP.	13	1
201501	2538	2015-01-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	But in terms of wage growth, I think the typical pattern is a slower response of wages relative to unemployment or output gaps.	13	1
201501	2539	2015-01-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	At least that’s my view of what we usually find in these Phillips curves.	13	1

201501	2550	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	President Kocherlakota.	13	1
201501	2551	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
201501	2552	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I’m going to respond briefly to some of the comments that President Lacker made about inflation compensation versus expectations.	13	1
201501	2553	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	You know, as the Federal Reserve Bank of Minneapolis has exposited in a recent memo, there is a distinction.	13	1
201501	2554	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	We actually think that that distinction favors the usage of compensation as opposed to trying to tease out some magical expectation measure, one that I’m not sure exactly where it comes from, as opposed to actually relying on the hard data on compensation.	13	1
201501	2555	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Now, why do I say that?	13	1
201501	2556	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	It’s because of the fact that compensation measures are compounding two different things: one, where inflation is going to be going on an average basis going forward, and, two, in terms of what the real outcomes are that are going to be associated with the average for inflation.	13	1
201501	2557	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	When compensation declines, it could be either because the average inflation rate that people are expecting going forward is going down, or because they’re associating that expectation with a very poor outcome in terms of real activity.	13	1
201501	2558	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I think both of those should lead us to be more concerned about a low inflation outcome than we would be otherwise.	13	1
201501	2559	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	So I think there’s an argument to be made, that we’ve made in the past, for focusing on compensation measures as opposed to what’s a much more arduous measure of teasing out expectations.	13	1
201501	2560	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	With all of that said, I think there’s room for rich debate on those topics.	13	1
201501	2561	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I’d be very opposed to removing references to market-based compensation from the statement.	13	1
201501	2562	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I think we should, first, stick as much as possible to the factual statements in the first paragraph and, second, removing that just because those are moving downward at this current moment in time does not send the right kind of message about data dependence and transparency on the part of this Committee.	13	1
201501	2563	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Let me turn now to briefly discussing conditions in the Ninth District before returning to the national inflation picture.	13	1
201501	2564	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	President Fisher did a great job of talking through the oil picture in Texas, and the lower oil prices are also going to be affecting my District through the Bakken region of western North Dakota and eastern Montana, which has fewer people living in it than Texas.	13	1
201501	2565	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	We expect that the effects on investment and employment within western North Dakota and eastern Montana will become more noticeable as we move through 2015.	13	1
201501	2566	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	But the Ninth District economy remains strong.	13	1
201501	2567	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Right now the unemployment rate in Minnesota has fallen to 3.6 percent.	13	1
201501	2568	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	It is about 0.5 percentage point below its pre-recession low in 2006.	13	1
201501	2569	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Interestingly, at the same time, wage pressures remain moderate, even in low- unemployment locales like the Twin Cities, whose unemployment rate is 3.2 percent.	13	1
201501	2570	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	These observations are consistent with a number of different hypotheses.	13	1
201501	2571	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	One is that the natural rate of unemployment in Minnesota may be no higher, or may even be lower, than it was prior to the Great Recession.	13	1
201501	2572	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Another alternative is, as President Williams was making reference to, that wage rigidities were more significant in the wake of this particular recessionary episode.	13	1
201501	2573	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	And another possibility is that inflation expectations have declined.	13	1
201501	2574	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	They would all push in the same direction of constraining wage growth.	13	1
201501	2575	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	This brings me to the national economy.	13	1
201501	2576	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	As many of you have noted in past meetings, 2014 was an outstanding year in terms of improvements in the labor market.	13	1
201501	2577	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Despite those improvements, however, the inflation outlook did not improve, and by some measures deteriorated significantly.	13	1
201501	2578	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I’m not talking here about the near-term outlook, which is naturally being dragged down by the unexpected decline in the prices of energy goods and services.	13	1
201501	2579	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The issue is that well after those oil price effects wane, Tealbook A, projects that inflation will still rise very slowly, not returning to target until 2019.	13	1
201501	2580	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Furthermore, as the minutes from our previous meeting note, the key assumption in the inflation forecast in Tealbook A, is that long- run inflation expectations, which the staff currently estimate to be 1¾ percent, will rise back to the Committee’s goal of 2 percent.	13	1
201501	2581	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	So a key underlying aspect of the staff’s forecast right now is that longer-run inflation expectations are at 1¾ percent, but they will rise back to the Committee’s goal of 2 percent over time.	13	1
201501	2582	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	This is a fine benchmark assumption, and I don’t quarrel with it, but I don’t think we can take this assumption for granted.	13	1
201501	2583	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Except for a couple of months in early 2012, PCE core inflation has been below 2 percent for more than six years.	13	1
201501	2584	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Headline inflation has been below 2 percent for more than two and a half years, and here I’m referring to the 12-month trailing moving average.	13	1
201501	2585	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Elsewhere around the world, disinflationary trends seem strong.	13	1
201501	2586	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	All of these observations have the potential to create downside risk to the credibility of our inflation target—that is, doubts in the mind of the public about the inflation rate we will actually achieve in the longer run.	13	1
201501	2587	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	In my view, we cannot be confident about the staff’s assumption with regard to inflation expectations unless our actions display a clear desire to return inflation to target.	13	1
201501	2588	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The behavior of financial market prices over the past six months and, really, especially over the past month reinforces these credibility concerns.	13	1
201501	2589	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	In particular, market-based five-year, five-year-ahead inflation expectations or compensation fell further to new lows over the intermeeting period.	13	1
201501	2590	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The premiums that option traders are paying to insure themselves against low inflation have also risen.	13	1
201501	2591	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	It is true that survey measures of long-term inflation expectations remain within their historical ranges, and that is comforting.	13	1
201501	2592	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	But if we think about this as a signal extraction problem, the overall signal about the credibility of our inflation target from both kinds of measures of longer-term inflation expectations has to be seen as sobering.	13	1
201501	2593	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Many of you have suggested that the recent gratifying improvement in labor market performance justifies further tightening of monetary policy even though inflation has remained muted.	13	1
201501	2594	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	But I see credibility risks in basing our monetary policy choices too much on the evolution of the real economy.	13	1
201501	2595	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	One way to interpret the monetary policy events of the 1960s and the 1970s is that the public became convinced that the FOMC was basing its policy choices largely on the evolution of employment rather than on the evolution of inflation.	13	1
201501	2596	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	As a result, the public lost confidence in the FOMC’s willingness to take systematic actions to keep inflation low, and inflation expectations became unanchored to the upside.	13	1
201501	2597	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Fast forward to 2015, and the FOMC is contemplating tightening policy in the absence of inflationary pressures simply because employment has risen, quite smartly, for the past year.	13	1
201501	2598	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Such a decision may suggest to the public that the FOMC is, again, basing its decision primarily on the real economy, as opposed to nominal measures.	13	1
201501	2599	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	And, just as in the 1960s and 1970s, this focus on the real side creates a risk of unanchoring inflation expectations, but this time to the downside as opposed to the upside.	13	1
201501	2600	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I’ll have more to say about our policy choices tomorrow, Madam Chair.	13	1

201501	2602	2015-01-28	meeting	LACKER	Madam Chair.	13	1
201501	2603	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	Yes.	13	1
201501	2604	2015-01-28	meeting	LACKER	If I could just offer a brief and friendly rejoinder to my friend from Minneapolis.	13	1
201501	2605	2015-01-28	meeting	LACKER	The first thing to say is that I don’t feel that the inflation-expectations measure is any more magical than things like underutilization of labor or maximum employment or the longer-run normal rate of unemployment.	13	1
201501	2606	2015-01-28	meeting	LACKER	But on a more serious note, I commend the Minneapolis Fed for putting in print a very clear explanation for both a broad audience and a technical audience of the relationship between inflation compensation and inflation expectations.	13	1
201501	2607	2015-01-28	meeting	LACKER	I thought it was a great contribution.	13	1
201501	2608	2015-01-28	meeting	LACKER	It makes clear that inflation expectations are a simple weighted average where the weights are probabilities of future possible inflation rates.	13	1
201501	2609	2015-01-28	meeting	LACKER	Inflation compensation is the same thing, one in which the weights combine both the probability and the margin of utility or a version of the states of the world in which particular inflation rates take place.	13	1
201501	2610	2015-01-28	meeting	LACKER	I’m very sympathetic to the notion that deriving our behavior from the fundamentals of individuals’ welfare would lead us to take very seriously those margin utilities.	13	1
201501	2611	2015-01-28	meeting	LACKER	On the other hand, I view our consensus statement as basically making a statement about expected inflation, about what inflation itself is supposed to average, not the combination of inflation and a marginal utility.	13	1
201501	2612	2015-01-28	meeting	LACKER	I think it’s a little easier to picture, a little easier for people to imagine, easier for people to think about.	13	1
201501	2613	2015-01-28	meeting	LACKER	And to use an analogy I’ve used several times before, it’s easier to put into your retirement-planning software than it is to put in inflation compensation.	13	1
201501	2614	2015-01-28	meeting	LACKER	That motivates my focus on this.	13	1
201501	2615	2015-01-28	meeting	LACKER	But I just wanted to acknowledge, first of all, the contribution of the exposition and, in addition, the worthy point that the preferences of the people that make up our economy are important to take into account.	13	1
201501	2616	2015-01-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I will forgo the opportunity of offering a further rejoinder to the rejoinder.	13	1

201501	2664	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	Finally, the improving tenor of business sentiment seems to be generating greater appetite for business investment.	13	1
201501	2665	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	Capital expenditures for expansion are being mentioned across a broad swath of industries, most notably in the transportation sector.	13	1
201501	2666	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	My outlook is little changed from the December meeting and pretty much in line with the Tealbook.	13	1
201501	2667	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	With the strength evident in the second half of 2014, I’m assuming a GDP growth run rate of around 3 percent.	13	1
201501	2668	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	Both the recent incoming data and anecdotal reports have increased my confidence in that view.	13	1
201501	2669	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	Associated with this optimism about economic growth, I expect continuing payroll job strength and declining headline unemployment.	13	1
201501	2670	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	I continue to project the U-3 unemployment rate to reach 5.3 percent by year-end, consistent with the December SEP central tendency, and I would not be surprised if a U-3 reading at this level came earlier.	13	1
201501	2671	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	My outlook for prices has year-over-year headline PCE near zero at midyear.	13	1
201501	2672	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	I’m not expecting much of a pass-through of lower crude oil prices and declining gasoline and refined product prices on core inflation numbers.	13	1
201501	2673	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	But at the same time, I expect no real firming of core inflation readings over the coming months, and I am increasingly apprehensive about dismissing the behavior of market-based inflation expectations measures.	13	1
201501	2674	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	It’s evident to me that the discussion of the timing of liftoff in the go-round following this one reflects some sentiment in the Committee favoring June liftoff.	13	1
201501	2675	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	A projected June liftoff could imply the signaling of that potentiality in March.	13	1
201501	2676	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	I’m not very comfortable with that prospect given the Committee’s repeated promulgation of data dependency as our guiding light.	13	1
201501	2677	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	Between now and our March FOMC meeting, we will get really only one incremental monthly retail price report, the January CPI and PCE reports.	13	1
201501	2678	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	I’m not hopeful that this January data will clarify matters and bolster confidence that the inflation trend is soon to turn in the direction of our target.	13	1
201501	2679	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	So any forecast-based signals sent in March that start a two-meeting countdown to liftoff will rely on rather light evidence, in my view.	13	1
201501	2680	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	I think there is a good chance that it will feel more like a leap of faith than a data-informed forecast.	13	1
201501	2681	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	Let me end on what, for me, is a discomfiting thought.	13	1
201501	2682	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	I’m finding it difficult to reconcile the data on growth in employment with the data on wages and prices.	13	1
201501	2683	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	To say this differently, I see a puzzling and confusing disconnect between the strong real economy performance represented by top-line GDP growth and labor market data, on the one hand, and wage and price trends on the other.	13	1
201501	2684	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	I don’t think we can entirely rule out the possibility that an unusual and persistent state of the world is developing.	13	1
201501	2685	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	This is a legitimate question.	13	1
201501	2686	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	It’s not going to be resolved in any serious way by the next meeting.	13	1

201501	2697	2015-01-28	meeting	PLOSSER	Our manufacturing sector, however, seems to have experienced some loss of momentum similar to that experienced in the nation as a whole.	13	1
201501	2698	2015-01-28	meeting	PLOSSER	Because of some abnormal seasonal behavior, it’s probably best to average through December and January’s general activity indexes.	13	1
201501	2699	2015-01-28	meeting	PLOSSER	That average yields a value of 15.3, a number that’s consistent with continued moderate growth, but is off the peaks we saw earlier in the fall.	13	1
201501	2700	2015-01-28	meeting	PLOSSER	Also, the unemployment index nudged into negative territory in the most recent survey.	13	1

201501	2705	2015-01-28	meeting	PLOSSER	However, I think we have to recall that in the second and third quarters of this past year, we witnessed real GDP growth of 4.6 percent and 5.6 percent, respectively.	13	1
201501	2706	2015-01-28	meeting	PLOSSER	It should not be surprising that the fourth quarter has not kept up such a torrid pace.	13	1

201501	2708	2015-01-28	meeting	PLOSSER	Even with the softer fourth quarter, we are likely to observe the last three quarters of 2014 as showing above 4 percent GDP growth.	13	1
201501	2709	2015-01-28	meeting	PLOSSER	Regarding the national outlook, I’m still fairly confident that the economy will grow above trend in 2014 at about 3 percent.	13	1
201501	2710	2015-01-28	meeting	PLOSSER	A good deal of my optimism revolves around continued increasing strength in the labor market that’s reflected in almost all of the labor market data we have seen.	13	1
201501	2711	2015-01-28	meeting	PLOSSER	The strength in the labor markets, along with improved household access to credit and falling prices at the pump, imply that consumer spending will support somewhat above-trend growth.	13	1
201501	2712	2015-01-28	meeting	PLOSSER	There are, of course, some risks.	13	1

201501	2715	2015-01-28	meeting	PLOSSER	And while the stronger dollar may weaken exports, the overall magnitude of such trade channel effects within the US economy remains modest.	13	1
201501	2716	2015-01-28	meeting	PLOSSER	While headline inflation, as expected, has been pushed down by falling oil prices, we should look through such relative price movements in this regard as we’ve done in the past.	13	1
201501	2717	2015-01-28	meeting	PLOSSER	I’m comforted by the fact that alternative metrics of underlying inflation, which have already been mentioned—such as medians, trimmed means, and other statistics—have remained remarkably stable over this period.	13	1
201501	2718	2015-01-28	meeting	PLOSSER	That should be encouraging to us about what the future may hold.	13	1

201501	2769	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	I see quite a bit of difference between the situation in Europe, where inflation is falling and economic growth is very weak, and the situation in the United States, where an oil price shock is driving down measures of headline inflation and economic growth is increasing.	13	1

201501	2797	2015-01-28	meeting	BULLARD	The pleasantly surprising readings on labor market outcomes during the past two years would, under conventional monetary policy logic, be thought to have moved the Committee toward a more aggressive path regarding the policy rate normalization.	13	1

201501	2800	2015-01-28	meeting	BULLARD	In other words, labor markets packed a powerful positive surprise over the past two years and yet actual policy expectations have moved in a dovish direction.	13	1

201501	2811	2015-01-28	meeting	BULLARD	Coupled with the substantial decline in oil prices, I see two powerful tailwinds for the United States as we enter 2015 on the top of considerable momentum in the last three quarters of 2014.	13	1

201501	2814	2015-01-28	meeting	BULLARD	During the autumn of 2010 in the United States, for example, real yields declined, equity prices rose, inflation expectations increased, and the dollar weakened.	13	1

201501	2833	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	I must point out that my forecast is based on an assessment of appropriate monetary policy that is not very different from current market expectations regarding the federal funds rate; that is, it is lower than the Tealbook projection, and lower than the assessments of most people around the table.	13	1
201501	2834	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	Regarding my projection of a 5 percent unemployment rate in 2016, my staff has done a good deal of research on changes in labor force participation and its composition that suggests the natural rate of unemployment could easily be as low as 5 percent.	13	1
201501	2835	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	The current anemic wage growth and low inflation pressures reinforce this inference.	13	1
201501	2836	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	It seems evident to me that labor slack, as many have said, is notably larger than many infer simply from the unemployment rate being 5.6 percent.	13	1
201501	2837	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	So I don’t see my unemployment rate forecast as undershooting the natural rate over the projection period as much as the Tealbook does.	13	1
201501	2838	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	With regard more directly to pricing pressures, inflation developments since December have not been supportive of the assessment that inflation is headed back to 2 percent within an acceptable period of time.	13	1
201501	2839	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	My last SEP submission had inflation only slowly crawling back up to 2 percent sometime in 2018, and my inclination today would be to nudge that path down just a touch further.	13	1
201501	2840	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	Recent data on core inflation have been soft, and there is a good chance they are going to soften even further, because of the appreciation of the dollar and the prospect of some pass-through from energy prices.	13	1
201501	2841	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	Indeed, all else being equal—that’s a strong statement—the dollar’s appreciation delivers an extra degree of financial restrictiveness that acts as if monetary policy has already tightened a bit.	13	1
201501	2842	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	And as I already mentioned, nominal wage growth is far below where it should be if we were hitting our inflation target.	13	1
201501	2843	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	I find recent financial market signals to be especially worrisome.	13	1
201501	2844	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	And let me take this opportunity to thank the staff for putting together a very nice collection of memos on how the projections of financial market variables are put together in the Tealbook and the other analyses that they have done, in addition to all of the special memos about the policy tools and liftoff.	13	1
201501	2845	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	It’s a lot for all of us to go through, but it’s really valuable information, and I appreciate it.	13	1
201501	2846	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	Now, on financial markets, the Board’s measure of TIPS five-year, five-year-forward breakeven rates has declined to 1.8 percent.	13	1
201501	2847	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	And this is on a CPI basis, so on a PCE basis, it is more like 1.5 percent.	13	1
201501	2848	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	And this is for inflation 6 to 10 years ahead, so it’s not good news for a longer-run period beyond the medium term.	13	1
201501	2849	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	I agree strongly with President Kocherlakota’s decidedly mainstream analysis in terms of risk-neutral pricing.	13	1
201501	2850	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	I was on a panel three weeks ago at the Becker Friedman Institute, and it was a conversation in which Nobel Prize winner Lars Hansen was asking me questions.	13	1
201501	2851	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	He went out of his way to pick up on Narayana’s comments on risk-neutral pricing, and it was very interesting.	13	1
201501	2852	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	Either inflation expectations have dropped much lower or investors are viewing low inflation as being associated with dramatically more costly economic outcomes.	13	1
201501	2853	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	Either way, these data are flashing red.	13	1
201501	2854	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	Our recent FOMC statements seem to downweight these observations, and President Lacker was suggesting just a few minutes ago that we should weight them even lower in the statement.	13	1
201501	2855	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	Why should we ignore these signals?	13	1
201501	2856	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	I have several comments on this.	13	1
201501	2857	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	Let me start with a benign alternative, which I have to say I don’t particularly find compelling—namely, that these movements in breakeven rates could be just noise that we can safely ignore.	13	1
201501	2858	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	As I understand the Board model—and one of the contributors to it is now a staff member at the Federal Reserve Bank of Chicago—the liquidity premium between nominal and indexed Treasuries is a key part of the decline in breakevens.	13	1
201501	2859	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	But liquidity is just a convenient label for a latent variable in this analysis.	13	1
201501	2860	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	It sounds relatively benign with that type of label, but it could include other unknown or unmodeled factors.	13	1
201501	2861	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	And, in fact, looking at the way the Board staff described it, they used the term “term premiums,” which, as I understand it, also includes liquidity premiums.	13	1
201501	2862	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	Few models actually attempt to identify liquidity effects from changes in bid–ask spreads, market transaction data, or the like.	13	1
201501	2863	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	One model that does is the Federal Reserve Bank of New York’s term structure model, and it attributes very little of the recent declines in breakevens to its liquidity adjustment factor.	13	1
201501	2864	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	So there is a good chance that what the Board model and some other models benignly label as liquidity or other premiums are really something that could be a cause for concern.	13	1
201501	2865	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	Whatever its interpretation, the time-series properties of these estimated liquidity premiums suggest the effects on breakevens should be relatively transitory.	13	1
201501	2866	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	That is, the underlying assumption that is used to estimate these finds that they ought to be relatively transitory, and this is something that we can monitor.	13	1
201501	2867	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	It’s a positive, not a normative, statement.	13	1
201501	2868	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	So if we don’t see breakevens mean reverting over the next few meetings, we should rethink the role of such temporary factors and the weight we put on this benign interpretation of the decline in breakevens.	13	1
201501	2869	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	And, furthermore, there is a more problematic interpretation of these so-called liquidity premium developments, and one that could be persistent, namely that they could reflect a more persistent increased preference for safe assets.	13	1
201501	2870	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	This would be consistent with President Kocherlakota’s worrisome analysis, as I interpret it.	13	1
201501	2871	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	I think these are reasons not to downplay this data, but to understand them much more than we do.	13	1
201501	2872	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	Another not-so-benign possibility is that market inflation expectations actually are too low, and, indeed, some affine term structure models do project low inflation.	13	1
201501	2873	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	Term structure models require a number of assumptions to identify the pricing kernels.	13	1
201501	2874	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	Alternative implementations can give, and do give, different answers.	13	1
201501	2875	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	As I already mentioned, the Board analysis seems to be consistent with stable inflation expectations.	13	1
201501	2876	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	Another approach is the term structure model developed by Luca Benzoni at the Federal Reserve Bank of Chicago.	13	1
201501	2877	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	This analysis doesn’t make reference to the TIPS data at all.	13	1
201501	2878	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	It uses other assumptions and modeling to examine the same issues.	13	1
201501	2879	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	To assess inflation pressures more directly, this analysis relies more on the breakdown of inflation among food, energy, and core inflation to better capture the dynamic relationships between interest rates and inflation.	13	1
201501	2880	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	These model forecasts for five-year, five-year-forward inflation also have come down about 20 basis points over the past year.	13	1
201501	2881	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	More important, the model is projecting very low inflation for quite some time.	13	1
201501	2882	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	The three-year-ahead average PCE inflation forecast for December 2017 is 1.62 percent.	13	1
201501	2883	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	This is average inflation from December 2017 to December 2019.	13	1
201501	2884	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	That’s too low.	13	1
201501	2885	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	Our Chicago model also does not use surveys of longer-run inflation expectations in order to assess stability of inflation expectations.	13	1
201501	2886	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	In contrast, some analyses appeal to the stability of survey measures of inflation at longer horizons to achieve reasonably stable longer- run inflation projections.	13	1
201501	2887	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	I worry that survey measures of longer-horizon inflation expectations are like looking at a stopped clock.	13	1
201501	2888	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	Longer-run measures don’t move much, and I’m skeptical as to whether survey measures of longer-run inflation expectations move meaningfully at times of great risk, and that’s something that President Rosengren was mentioning with regard to Japan.	13	1
201501	2889	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	Or is it the case that the survey respondents see a looming risk, but they are confident that we, the FOMC, will act appropriately?	13	1
201501	2890	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	Do we even agree among ourselves on what these appropriate actions should be?	13	1
201501	2891	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	The divergence between market and survey measures gives me great concern.	13	1
201501	2892	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	Now, putting all of these models aside, no matter how you view the breakevens, we still need to grapple with the signal content simply coming from the very low level of the 10-year Treasury rate.	13	1
201501	2893	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	If real rates are low, then markets are expecting poorer economic outcomes over a longer term than our SEP paths likely embody.	13	1
201501	2894	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	If inflation expectations are low, then we likely have inadequate accommodation in place, and our SEP numbers are too optimistic on that score.	13	1
201501	2895	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	If it’s noise, well, okay, no worries, and I guess we can go ahead with planning policy normalization sooner rather than later.	13	1
201501	2896	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	But in my book, this adds up to only one out of three possibilities being acceptable.	13	1
201501	2897	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	And I just don’t like those odds.	13	1
201501	2898	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	To conclude, I think we’re most likely finally on a path to achieving the employment part of our mandate in a reasonable amount of time, but my outlook for inflation is nowhere near satisfactory.	13	1
201501	2899	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	From a risk-management standpoint, I just can’t see the argument for hurrying.	13	1
201501	2900	2015-01-28	meeting	EVANS	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1

201501	2905	2015-01-28	meeting	GEORGE	The effects of the decline in oil prices have, on net, been favorable, with nearly 50 percent of firms in our manufacturing and services surveys reporting a positive effect on their business, while around 20 percent report a negative effect.	13	1
201501	2906	2015-01-28	meeting	GEORGE	Of course, District states that are most dependent on energy production—Wyoming, Oklahoma, and New Mexico— face a somewhat less favorable outlook.	13	1
201501	2907	2015-01-28	meeting	GEORGE	Oil rig counts have started to decline rapidly, and capital spending is expected to be down considerably in 2015.	13	1
201501	2908	2015-01-28	meeting	GEORGE	Based on our quarterly energy survey, nearly half of respondents expect more than a 20 percent reduction in capital spending this year.	13	1
201501	2909	2015-01-28	meeting	GEORGE	Significant layoffs are already being announced at some District energy firms, and more are expected.	13	1
201501	2910	2015-01-28	meeting	GEORGE	Analysis of hedging and debt positions shows that firms headquartered in the 10th District, which account for about one- third of all large independents, are comparable to other US shale oil firms but with sizable differences across those firms.	13	1
201501	2911	2015-01-28	meeting	GEORGE	In general, the smaller firms are more highly leveraged, but also better hedged, than the large firms.	13	1
201501	2912	2015-01-28	meeting	GEORGE	Although most states now rely considerably less on the oil and gas extraction sector than 30 years ago, it is possible that energy-dependent states like Oklahoma could experience double-digit percentage declines in energy sector jobs, and perhaps slight declines in overall employment.	13	1
201501	2913	2015-01-28	meeting	GEORGE	For the nation as a whole, my outlook for economic activity is little changed from the previous meeting.	13	1
201501	2914	2015-01-28	meeting	GEORGE	Weaker foreign economic growth and a stronger dollar may weigh on GDP, but lower energy prices, on net, should provide a boost.	13	1
201501	2915	2015-01-28	meeting	GEORGE	As a result, I continue to anticipate moderate growth of around 3 percent this year.	13	1
201501	2916	2015-01-28	meeting	GEORGE	Consumer spending was strong in the second half of last year, and consumers’ assessments of current economic and labor market conditions have brightened.	13	1
201501	2917	2015-01-28	meeting	GEORGE	I expect low oil prices and further labor market improvements to provide ongoing support to household spending.	13	1
201501	2918	2015-01-28	meeting	GEORGE	The labor market has maintained its momentum, and my outlook for further improvement is optimistic based on the Federal Reserve Bank of Kansas City’s labor market conditions indicators, which showed that the level of activity improved again in December, and its momentum rose to its highest level over the past two decades.	13	1
201501	2919	2015-01-28	meeting	GEORGE	Of particular note, among the 24 labor market variables that are used to construct this index, the five strongest contributors to improvement over the past six months are predominantly leading indicators—a rise in the quits rate, a decline in those working part time for economic reasons, improved job flows from unemployment to employment, an increase in the percent of firms planning to increase employment, and a shift downward in the Blue Chip unemployment rate forecast.	13	1
201501	2920	2015-01-28	meeting	GEORGE	In an environment of above-trend economic growth and falling unemployment, of course, inflation remains low.	13	1
201501	2921	2015-01-28	meeting	GEORGE	Clearly, lower oil prices and the stronger dollar will exert some downward pressure in the near term, but these are relative-price movements that should prove temporary and not affect the longer-term inflation outlook.	13	1
201501	2922	2015-01-28	meeting	GEORGE	For this reason, the recent decline in market-based measures looks puzzling, as others have discussed, and I agree that this is a development that we will need to watch.	13	1
201501	2923	2015-01-28	meeting	GEORGE	While inflation is running below our stated quantitative objective, it is less clear to me at this point that the goal of price stability over the longer run is at risk.	13	1
201501	2924	2015-01-28	meeting	GEORGE	Furthermore, I do not think we can judge the cost of low inflation in isolation from other economic developments.	13	1
201501	2925	2015-01-28	meeting	GEORGE	Inflation below target in an environment of above-trend economic growth and falling unemployment seems less of a concern to me than low inflation combined with slowing economic growth and rising unemployment.	13	1
201501	2926	2015-01-28	meeting	GEORGE	As oil prices and the dollar stabilize, my forecast implies that inflation will move upward, and spare capacity and tightening of labor markets will gradually lift inflation back toward target.	13	1
201501	2927	2015-01-28	meeting	GEORGE	So unless signs emerge that the economy’s performance is faltering, I see no need to delay normalization for the purpose of accelerating a return to 2 percent.	13	1
