201501	2933	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	I view the incoming data as consistent with an economy that continues to grow at an above-trend pace, marked by rising household and business confidence.	13	1
201501	2934	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	While it’s true there have been some data points that have raised some questions—for example, for December, we had a weak retail sales report and a decline in average hourly earnings—I view these as likely to be anomalies that will be reversed in the coming months.	13	1
201501	2935	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	One question I have been wondering about is the timing of the effect of the decline on oil prices on US economic activity.	13	1
201501	2936	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	We have the positive benefit of lower oil prices boosting real income, and this factor appears to be supporting consumer spending.	13	1
201501	2937	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	But, against this, we have the prospect for a significant decline in oil and gas investment.	13	1
201501	2938	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	Although I agree with the conclusion that the net effect is a positive, I wonder about the timing in which these effects fall.	13	1
201501	2939	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	Does the boost in consumer spending happen first, followed by the drop in investment, or not?	13	1
201501	2940	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	I think understanding the likely timing here will help us better assess the trajectory of economic growth.	13	1
201501	2941	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	If it is consumption first as a positive, and investment later as a negative, which would be my own expectation in terms of relative timing, that suggests that there is a risk that the momentum to economic growth fades as we get deeper into the year.	13	1
201501	2942	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	I raise this issue as it might be relevant to thinking about the appropriate timing of monetary policy liftoff.	13	1
201501	2943	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	Another key issue for me is the trend in wages and labor compensation.	13	1
201501	2944	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	If growth of nominal wages and labor compensation stays steady or firms in the face of declining headline inflation, then this implies more real income growth, all else being equal.	13	1
201501	2945	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	And it also implies that the lower headline inflation rate or the drop in market-based measures of inflation compensation are not having strong depressing effects on the wage-setting process.	13	1
201501	2946	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	In terms of labor compensation, some of the survey measures I have seen recently imply strengthening rather than weakening.	13	1
201501	2947	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	For example, the Federal Reserve Bank of New York’s consumer expectations survey indicates a generally firming trend for one year ahead in terms of household income growth expectations.	13	1
201501	2948	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	And in the same vein, the most recent National Federation of Independent Business survey showed a firming compensation trend, with a net 25 percent of firms reporting paying higher compensation—that’s an increase of 4 points from their prior monthly survey— and a net 17 percent planning to raise compensation in coming months.	13	1
201501	2949	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	That’s up 2 points over the prior survey.	13	1
201501	2950	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	The second issue that I’m focused on is the level of full employment.	13	1
201501	2951	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	In the last SEP round, I lowered my estimate of the longer-run unemployment rate to 5 percent.	13	1
201501	2952	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	I think there is some risk that this estimate is still too high.	13	1
201501	2953	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	There are several reasons why my estimate is coming down.	13	1
201501	2954	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	First, demographic trends in the workforce are consistent with a lower longer-run unemployment rate.	13	1
201501	2955	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	The composition of the workforce is getting older, and this should be pushing down the longer-run unemployment rate because older age cohorts typically have lower unemployment rates.	13	1
201501	2956	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	I asked my staff to isolate how much lower the current full-employment unemployment rate would be, compared with its value in earlier periods, if the only thing that had changed was the age composition of the labor force.	13	1
201501	2957	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	If we go all the way back to 1980, the net effect of the changing age composition of the labor force is worth nearly a full percentage point in the longer-run unemployment rate.	13	1
201501	2958	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	Nearer at hand, comparing the situation with 2004, which was when in that cycle the unemployment rate first reached 5.6 percent, the same level as it is now, the shift is worth about 0.2 percentage point.	13	1
201501	2959	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	Second, the national full-employment unemployment rate depends on what is happening in the labor markets in different regions.	13	1
201501	2960	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	If there’s a lot of dispersion in labor markets, some very tight and others very loose, then this should lead to a higher national full-employment unemployment rate, all else being equal.	13	1
201501	2961	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	With the drop in oil prices likely to hurt disproportionately those states that have higher labor demand and lower unemployment rates, I would expect the dispersion of labor market conditions across the country should lessen, and this should directionally lower the longer-run unemployment rate a bit.	13	1
201501	2962	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	We tried to estimate how much this might be worth, and it turns out this effect is likely to be quite small—no more than 0.1 percentage point.	13	1
201501	2963	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	Third, as we all know, the unemployment rate is not a sufficient statistic of labor market slack.	13	1
201501	2964	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	To the extent that the current unemployment rate understates the degree of slack in the labor market, it also understates the degree of slack relative to the full-employment unemployment rate.	13	1
201501	2965	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	I think this is very much the case today.	13	1
201501	2966	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	To assess this effect, one can measure the degree to which there is underutilization of the labor force—for example, part-time workers who want to work full time—relative to the unemployment rate.	13	1
201501	2967	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	In this cycle, the unemployment rate is unusually low compared with the amount of slack measured by those who want to work more hours.	13	1
201501	2968	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	If we normalize the underutilization of hours in relation to past cycles, this would imply an unemployment rate of about 6.1 percent currently, so that’s a gap of 0.5 percentage point.	13	1
201501	2969	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	The final factor that might be relevant is the fact that labor compensation and inflation trends are unusually low at the current unemployment rate.	13	1
201501	2970	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	Real total compensation based on the core PCE inflation rate and the employment cost index is only running at about an annual rate of ½ percent.	13	1
201501	2971	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	Now, I’m not sure what this precisely means in terms of how much slack we have and what that means for the full-employment unemployment rate, but when the labor compensation trends are this low, it would seem to allow us the ability to be somewhat more patient.	13	1
201501	2972	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	A third issue that I’ve been thinking a lot about is the outlook for foreign economic growth and the dollar.	13	1
201501	2973	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	Here I have a mixed view.	13	1
201501	2974	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	On the one hand, and I think this is consistent with Steve Kamin’s briefing, the pessimism about the outlook for growth abroad looks a bit overdone, especially with respect to Europe and Japan.	13	1
201501	2975	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	When I look at both Europe and Japan, while I see plenty of problems, I also see a number of positives, including lower oil prices, weaker currencies, more expansive monetary policies, and less fiscal drag.	13	1
201501	2976	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	The ECB’s QE initiative has gotten good reviews so far.	13	1
201501	2977	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	And most noteworthy to me was Mario Draghi’s comment that the program will continue until the ECB expects its inflation objectives to be achieved.	13	1
201501	2978	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	The fact that it’s open ended is, I think, very, very important.	13	1

201501	2980	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	QE in both Japan and Europe certainly would seem to push in this direction.	13	1
201501	2981	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	I understand the reasons for the Tealbook’s forecast for the dollar.	13	1
201501	2982	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	If it was obvious that the dollar would continue to appreciate, then it would already have happened.	13	1
201501	2983	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	Nevertheless, I view the risk of a much stronger dollar as the biggest risk to my forecast of above-trend economic growth and rising inflation.	13	1
201501	2984	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	So that’s the biggest risk to what forms the foundation of my expectations that we can safely lift off with respect to monetary policy later this year.	13	1
201501	2985	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	A firmer dollar is something I am definitely concerned about as a risk to our forecast.	13	1

201501	2999	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	It will have an effect on investment, but housing is beginning to show some signs of growth—it’s nice having a sector that you expected to grow and doesn’t grow, because you know one day it will grow, and then it will contribute to economic growth.	13	1
201501	3000	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	I want to say a word about our estimates of the natural rate of unemployment.	13	1
201501	3001	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	I’m going to say something I’ve said here before, and I apologize, but I think it happens to be true, so I’ll keep saying it.	13	1
201501	3002	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	If you look at what economists say the natural rate of unemployment is and run a regression on it—and when I say “economists,”	13	1
201501	3003	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	I’m usually talking about Bob Gordon, who at some stage used to produce an estimate of the natural rate of unemployment with sufficient frequency that you could run regressions on it—what you find is that the stated natural rate is 4.5 percent plus half the gap between the actual unemployment rate and 4.5 percent.	13	1
201501	3004	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	So poor Vice Chairman Dudley worked very hard to come to roughly—[laughter]—that estimate.	13	1
201501	3005	2015-01-28	meeting	DUDLEY	Could have saved a lot of work.	13	1
201501	3006	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	Yes.	13	1
201501	3007	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	There is a tendency to reduce our estimate of the natural rate as the unemployment rate comes down, but at some point that stops.	13	1
201501	3008	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	It will be at 4.5 percent, 4.8 percent maybe.	13	1
201501	3009	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	Bill is right that it’s now about 5 percent.	13	1
201501	3010	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	I want to talk next about foreign activity and how to think about that.	13	1
201501	3011	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	China has slowed down.	13	1
201501	3012	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	That has a much bigger effect on the world than you might have guessed.	13	1
201501	3013	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	It’s affecting Australia, it’s affecting Latin America, and if you go down the list of countries that produce metals and other commodities—Brazil, Chile, and all the others—you’ll find they’re all being hit by the slowdown in China.	13	1
201501	3014	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	Now, you know, it is a slowdown from 10 percent to 7 percent, and it seems funny to call it a slowdown, but that’s what it is.	13	1
201501	3015	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	We’ve also got to worry about Greece because we’re certainly going to get a lot of alarm bells ringing about Greece, even if the likelihood is that it turns out to stay in Europe.	13	1
201501	3016	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	We’ve got Ukraine, and that one is really a potentially huge mess, and we’ve got a war in the Middle East, which is getting very ugly.	13	1
201501	3017	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	But we do now have the ECB having undertaken this very brave and very difficult set of measures, which Mario Draghi managed to get through his executive board.	13	1
201501	3018	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	And we’ve also got the Japanese trying to restore their economic growth.	13	1
201501	3019	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	If you ask what is happening in our capital markets, I think it’s that interest rates are very low everywhere else, and people don’t know about covered interest arbitrage or they don’t believe in it, one or the other.	13	1
201501	3020	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	So they’re coming here, because exchange rates apparently never change in their minds.	13	1
201501	3021	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	And that’s, I think, an element that is driving our rates down.	13	1
201501	3022	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	The major problem for us is the appreciation.	13	1
201501	3023	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	As we saw in the Tealbook, its effects about two years out outweigh the effects of the benefit from oil, assuming the exchange rate stays roughly where it is now and oil prices stay roughly where they are.	13	1
201501	3024	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	They probably won’t.	13	1
201501	3025	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	They’ll probably go up some.	13	1
201501	3026	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	So now, having talked about lower interest rates, we need to go to inflation.	13	1
201501	3027	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	Well, what are we looking at?	13	1
201501	3028	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	According to the Tealbook, core inflation was 1 percent last quarter and will be 1.8 percent in the second half of 2015.	13	1
201501	3029	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	They have it at 1.7 percent at the end of 2016 and 1.9 percent at the end of 2017.	13	1
201501	3030	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	So what do we do, and how do we think about that?	13	1
201501	3031	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	Well, I have to think about the consensus statement all the time, and that tells us what we should do.	13	1
201501	3032	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	We should have a balanced approach.	13	1
201501	3033	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	The balanced approach is essentially summarized by the utility function, which weights losses, the square of the divergence between inflation and target inflation squared and unemployment and the natural rate squared.	13	1
201501	3034	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	That has a nice, smooth tradeoff, and it will give you something like the Taylor rule when combined with a model.	13	1
201501	3035	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	It has inflation with a positive sign on the interest rate and unemployment with a negative sign on the interest rate, and you trade the two of them off.	13	1
201501	3036	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	Now, what I’ve been listening to for some time is a different utility function.	13	1
201501	3037	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	It’s called the Leontief utility function, which says that our utility is the minimum of the difference between inflation and expected inflation and unemployment and the natural rate—that is, if unemployment is at the natural rate, we can’t move until the inflation rate gets to the target rate of 2 percent.	13	1
201501	3038	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	That’s how the conversation is going, but, in fact, our consensus statement says we trade things off.	13	1
201501	3039	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	In reading through all the material that came through, I noticed, quite a few times, more implicit acceptance of the view that we’re going to have to push unemployment below the natural rate in order to get inflation up.	13	1
201501	3040	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	And that seems to be something that changed—and I’ll ask the staff afterward to explain how that happened—because a few months ago several people here were dead-set against that possibility.	13	1
201501	3041	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	But I think there’s a tradeoff that what we say in the consensus statement would require us to take into account, and it wouldn’t necessarily say you can’t do anything about the interest rate at this point.	13	1
201501	3042	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	In fact, that tradeoff says it will give more weight to the unemployment rate.	13	1
201501	3043	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	At some point when we see any signs of progress, when the unemployment rate is going down below the natural rate, we will be beginning to look at raising the interest rate.	13	1
201501	3044	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	So what are we going to do?	13	1
201501	3045	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	Well, we’re faced with a very tough choice, but I don’t think we should proceed on the basis of thinking we can’t move at all until the inflation rate hits 2 percent, which is roughly what I’ve been hearing.	13	1
201501	3046	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	And I know that this is unpopular, but I think we do not know the difference between 1.8 percent and 2 percent inflation in terms of its effect on the welfare of the representative consumer in the United States.	13	1
201501	3047	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	And it’s true we wrote down 2 percent.	13	1
201501	3048	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	I would have been more comfortable working with a range of the inflation rate, because I think our lack of knowledge of what precisely is the relationship between the inflation rate and utility of ordinary individuals is quite considerable.	13	1
201501	3049	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	We will make those decisions.	13	1
201501	3050	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	We will discuss those tradeoffs further tomorrow.	13	1
201501	3051	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	And I do want to add one thing.	13	1
201501	3052	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	I’m very uncomfortable working at the effective lower bound.	13	1
201501	3053	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	I know that everybody says we can’t move from here, but my guess is we can move from here.	13	1
201501	3054	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	And you say, “We’ll go back to the effective lower bound with high probability.”	13	1
201501	3055	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	First of all, it’s not high probability in the very excellent analytic work we’ve been given, and using the FRB/US model to run stochastic simulations tells us that it doesn’t make much difference if you go sooner or go later.	13	1
201501	3056	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	We’ll have to discuss this tomorrow, but I suggest we don’t discuss it in terms of the idea that there is no tradeoff until you’re right at the point of inflation at 2 percent and unemployment at the natural rate.	13	1
201501	3057	2015-01-28	meeting	FISCHER	Thank you.	13	1

201501	3099	2015-01-28	meeting	TARULLO	And this is obviously really important, because, as I understand it, any significant difference in the natural rate plugged into any one of the various models that are used results in a quite different timing of liftoff and differences in what you do with regard to monetary policy thereafter.	13	1

201501	3101	2015-01-28	meeting	TARULLO	To some degree, the possibility that the natural rate is lower is the flip side of the low labor force participation rate relative to what we would have expected with demographic trends, which is to say, lower, but not as low as it has been.	13	1
201501	3102	2015-01-28	meeting	TARULLO	That is, people who are currently categorized as unemployed may be more attached to the workforce and more skilled than the norm for unemployed workers historically.	13	1
201501	3103	2015-01-28	meeting	TARULLO	In addition, a lot of people who would have formerly been listed as unemployed, but who are actually less tied to or qualified for work, may now be listed as out of the labor force.	13	1

201501	3105	2015-01-28	meeting	TARULLO	Now, that’s an intuition to some degree, or a theoretical possibility, but it is buttressed by the fact that, contrary to expectations, the exit rate from unemployment to out of the labor force has actually fallen relative to what it was pre-crisis, and entry into the labor force—again, taking demographics into account—has been low.	13	1

201501	3152	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	And with initial claims remaining at a low level and job openings remaining elevated, it seems likely that we are going to continue to see gains close to this magnitude in the first part of this year.	13	1
201501	3153	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	That should continue to push the unemployment rate down and should continue to put enough upward pressure on the participation rate to keep it from falling despite the downward structural trend.	13	1
201501	3154	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	I think recent indicators of aggregate spending also support that growth in labor demand despite the surprising drop in December retail sales.	13	1
201501	3155	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	The data on consumer spending has been, on balance, positive, and further declines in energy prices, along with a substantial step-up in consumer sentiment, should support continued strong consumer spending, enough to more than compensate for the drop that President Fisher talked about earlier in drilling and mining investment.	13	1
201501	3156	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Investment spending is a little bit more of a mixed picture, but December’s data on housing starts were the most favorable we have seen in some time.	13	1
201501	3157	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Despite that improvement, particularly in the labor market, we have seen no broad-based acceleration of wages, and that is a puzzle.	13	1
201501	3158	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	The news on inflation has also been dominated by continued declines, particularly from oil prices.	13	1
201501	3159	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	While I think we should expect to see the effect of these declines to be temporary, I am concerned about the possibility that deflationary shocks may continue for some time and eventually feed through to expectations.	13	1
201501	3160	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	The news on core inflation was a bit softer than expected, and the annualized three-month change in core PCE prices is likely to be below 1 percent in December.	13	1
201501	3161	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Survey-based measures, as many have pointed out, have held steady, a development that holds out the hope that, when the effect of oil and dollar shocks subside, and as resource slack continues to diminish, inflation should move up toward 2 percent.	13	1
201501	3162	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	But while I am mostly comfortable looking through the sharp decline in oil prices, I am increasingly uncomfortable doing the same with regard to market-based measures of inflation compensation, which have continued to decline.	13	1
201501	3163	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Because those measures are likely more forward looking than survey-based measures, I don’t think we can dismiss the possibility that the risks to inflation and inflation expectations are on the downside.	13	1
201501	3164	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Related to that, I have been somewhat preoccupied with the significant decline in longer- term nominal interest rates, which is striking at a time when this Committee is signaling the approach of liftoff.	13	1
201501	3165	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	In particular, the 10-year Treasury has declined close to 1 percentage point over the past year.	13	1
201501	3166	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Two aspects of that decline are noteworthy.	13	1
201501	3167	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	First, it’s concentrated farther out along the yield curve, and, second, changes in the expected path of short-term rates do not appear to account for much of the change—or, put differently, it has been concentrated in the term premium.	13	1
201501	3168	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	I think understanding why this decline is happening is important for our policy deliberations tomorrow, and in this regard I think two explanations merit particular attention.	13	1
201501	3169	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	First, the one that has been cited here:  Extremely low longer-term interest rates in advanced foreign economies and concerns about a weaker global economic outlook may be exerting downward pressure on yields.	13	1
201501	3170	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	That one, I think, is a slightly easier scenario to deal with.	13	1
201501	3171	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	But the second is that there is some evidence that market participants may have materially altered the probability they assign to low inflation outcomes and weak economic conditions.	13	1
201501	3172	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Previously, when market participants appeared to be most concerned about the risk of higher inflation coinciding with unfavorable macroeconomic conditions, nominal Treasury securities had to yield more in order for investors to hold them.	13	1
201501	3173	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	More recently, this concern appears to have been replaced by the concern that low inflation will coincide with unfavorable macroeconomic conditions.	13	1
201501	3174	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	In these states, nominal Treasury securities have attractive returns.	13	1
201501	3175	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	This consideration could explain why the term premium has declined.	13	1
201501	3176	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	This scenario was laid out very nicely for us in an earlier memo from President Kocherlakota, and it is given some weight in the chart in the Tealbook that shows a striking shift in market-based measures toward a higher probability being placed on low inflation outcomes.	13	1
201501	3177	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Finally and related again, there is something that many have commented on, the strong activity domestically comes against a divergent backdrop of weak foreign economic growth and deflationary pressures from abroad.	13	1
201501	3178	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Indeed, the one sector of the US economy other than oil exploration in which prospects remain distinctly unfavorable is net exports.	13	1
201501	3179	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Since last summer, the dollar has increased about 10 percent.	13	1
201501	3180	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Staff models suggest such an increase will eventually subtract nearly 2 percent from the level of GDP, and the contribution of net exports to GDP growth in the Tealbook averages a subtraction of about ½ percentage point over the next few years.	13	1
201501	3181	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	The size and the open-ended nature of the ECB’s policy announcement on purchases of sovereigns surprised markets and sent a definitive signal that the ECB is prepared to take the necessary action to prevent deflation in the euro area, which is welcome.	13	1
201501	3182	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	But challenges remain.	13	1
201501	3183	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	The extent to which this recent action coincides with a stronger recovery in euro-area economic growth will depend on strengthening confidence about the outlook for activity and inflation, the emergence of stronger domestic demand, and the absence of further threats to the cohesion of the euro area.	13	1
201501	3184	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Upcoming negotiations between Greece and its creditors following the Syriza victory on Sunday will test the last of these.	13	1
201501	3185	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	And, more generally, continued disagreement among euro- area members about the extent of needed structural reforms and fiscal support are likely to continue to weigh on the recovery.	13	1
201501	3186	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Developments beyond Europe are also not hugely encouraging.	13	1
201501	3187	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Even if a deceleration in China’s economic growth occurs smoothly, the deceleration itself is likely to contribute to continued global deflationary pressures for some time.	13	1
201501	3188	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	The Tealbook projection, I think, involves an assumption that the dollar does not move further.	13	1
201501	3189	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	My own assessment is that the risks associated with that forecast are in the direction of further dollar appreciation.	13	1
201501	3190	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Indeed, as we have seen, in response to last Thursday’s ECB action, the dollar has already appreciated since the Tealbook was finalized.	13	1
201501	3191	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	So, in sum, I think we can expect some persistence in deflationary pressures from abroad with dollar strength outweighing possible improvements in foreign economic growth.	13	1
201501	3192	2015-01-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
201501	3193	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	13	1

201501	3196	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	I apologize in advance that I won’t do justice to all of the interesting and detailed analysis that some of you have presented.	13	1
201501	3197	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	And then I’d like to add a couple of remarks of my own.	13	1
201501	3198	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	Starting with the labor market, I think we all agree that labor market conditions continue to improve, and most of us seem optimistic that further improvement is likely in the period ahead.	13	1
201501	3199	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	The labor market report for December may not have quite lived up to the sizzling standards of November, but it certainly provided further evidence that the recent trend in employment has become stronger, appreciably stronger, than it was earlier in the year.	13	1
201501	3200	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	The unemployment rate fell a further 0.2 percentage point, which is more than we expected, and broader measures of utilization have similarly moved down.	13	1
201501	3201	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	Both the Board and the Kansas City indexes of labor market conditions have picked up.	13	1
201501	3202	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	Even so, a number of you noted that there likely remains an appreciable margin of slack in the labor market despite these recent gains.	13	1
201501	3203	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	The Tealbook, for example, emphasizes that the unemployment gap at this point appears to understate the true amount of labor market slack, in the sense that the output gap is currently estimated to be significantly larger than that consistent with Okun’s law, reflecting the cyclical shortfall in labor force participation and involuntary part-time employment.	13	1
201501	3204	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	Of course, the behavior of labor compensation also provides some information on labor market slack.	13	1
201501	3205	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	And as David noted in his presentation and as shown in the Tealbook simulation, it has important implications for inflation.	13	1
201501	3206	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	Most measures of labor compensation still haven’t moved upward in any convincing way.	13	1
201501	3207	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	The average hourly earnings number moved in the wrong direction.	13	1
201501	3208	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	And while that may be noise, we still really haven’t seen any meaningful pickup in wage growth.	13	1
201501	3209	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	Several of you noted that surveys hint that wage growth is about to pick up, but that’s something we haven’t yet seen.	13	1
201501	3210	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	I suppose one response to this that I heard from several of you around the table is that you are beginning to revise down your estimates of the longer-run unemployment rate.	13	1
201501	3211	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	This is something, as Governor Tarullo mentioned, that also is perhaps a logical reaction to the notion that at this point more slack may be showing up in other forms, whether it’s part-time employment or individuals who are not working but not actively looking and are, therefore, counted as out of the labor force.	13	1
201501	3212	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	That may be another factor that has led to a downward revision to estimates of the longer-run unemployment rate, and I would add that I also am in the camp of those who, for all these reasons, have recently revised down my own estimate.	13	1
201501	3213	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	Of course, it’s also important to remember, as President Williams emphasized in his comments, that the behavior of wages may continue to be influenced by downward nominal wage rigidity.	13	1
201501	3214	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	If firms were constrained from cutting wages during the recession, they may face less pressure to raise them now than would be expected on the basis of the existing degree of labor market slack.	13	1
201501	3215	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	In other words, a stock of pent-up wage deflation may currently be holding wage increases down.	13	1
201501	3216	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	But as this source of restraint erodes, we could see nonlinearities emerge in the behavior of nominal wage growth and price inflation, and this is something that President Fisher has emphasized.	13	1
201501	3217	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	I thought the Tealbook had an interesting box this time that discussed the implications of this type of view.	13	1
201501	3218	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	Turning to recent developments in the broader economy, most of you seem reasonably confident that real GDP will continue expanding at a solid pace, thereby enabling further improvements in the labor market.	13	1
201501	3219	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	You noted that real GDP likely expanded at a rapid pace, maybe about 3¾ percent or so, in the second half of last year, and that the growth of consumer spending was quite brisk.	13	1
201501	3220	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	And many of you expect this strength in domestic spending to continue this year, with household purchasing power having been markedly boosted by declining gasoline prices.	13	1
201501	3221	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	Several of you also noted a significant improvement in consumer confidence.	13	1
201501	3222	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	But, as President Lacker noted, while the boost to consumer spending from declining gasoline prices should prove transitory, employment growth and the associated income growth and gains in wealth are ongoing factors supporting consumer spending.	13	1
201501	3223	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	Business confidence also seems quite strong.	13	1
201501	3224	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	We had a good discussion around the table of the effect of lower oil prices.	13	1
201501	3225	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	I think there was general agreement that, on balance, it should be positive, but it’s really difficult to figure out exactly what effect it is likely to have with respect to capital spending and employment in the energy sector.	13	1
201501	3226	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	It was good to hear from Presidents Fisher and Kocherlakota that they see less likelihood than was the case in the 1980s that these declines will have very serious negative effects on their regions and on, for example, banking conditions.	13	1
201501	3227	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	But as Vice Chairman Dudley pointed out, the timing with regard to when we see effects on investment spending and employment in the energy sector relative to the boost to spending is really a significant factor for the forecast going forward.	13	1

201501	3231	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	Many of you mentioned that the steep appreciation we have seen in the dollar is likely to weaken net exports in the years ahead by exerting a larger influence over time than the positive effects on spending from lower oil prices.	13	1
201501	3232	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	And as some of you noted, further appreciation of the dollar is certainly possible and such an eventuality presents a downside risk to the forecast.	13	1
201501	3233	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	With respect to financial conditions, we had some discussion of why longer-term interest rates are so low.	13	1

201501	3235	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	One possibility is that we are simply seeing spillovers from the rest of the world, which has seen an expectation of quantitative easing and has had weak economic conditions.	13	1
201501	3236	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	And if that’s the main scenario, that would seem to be a positive factor for our national forecast.	13	1
201501	3237	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	But as several of you noted, other scenarios or explanations are also possible, in particular that the low level of rates may reflect low inflation expectations or expectations of weak economic growth.	13	1
201501	3238	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	On the inflation front, incoming data suggest that we are making somewhat slower progress towards our 2 percent goal than previously anticipated.	13	1
201501	3239	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	The core CPI inflation rate in December was unchanged.	13	1
201501	3240	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	The staff has revised down its core PCE inflation estimate in the fourth quarter to 1.1 percent from 1.6 percent at the time of the previous meeting.	13	1
201501	3241	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	The staff currently anticipates that core PCE inflation will continue to run close to 1 percent during the winter but pick up a bit in the spring, and that’s a downward revision.	13	1
201501	3242	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	And some of you noted in your own discussions that you have also adjusted downward your forecast of PCE inflation and are increasingly worried that inflation may remain below 2 percent for some time.	13	1
201501	3243	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	Some of you noted that the rise of the dollar since our previous meeting will put additional downward pressure on prices of imported goods and other commodities—and thereby lower readings on inflation during the first half of this year by more than you previously expected—and that the further decline we have seen in oil prices may also spill over into lower core inflation.	13	1
201501	3244	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	I think it’s fair to say that most of you emphasized that you don’t expect these disinflationary forces to have a persistent effect on inflation beyond 2015, particularly in light of the continued stability of survey measures of long-run inflation.	13	1
201501	3245	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	But a number of you certainly expressed concern that inflation expectations may move down, and that we are likely to see little or no evidence in the next several months that will improve our confidence that inflation is going to move back to 2 percent.	13	1
201501	3246	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	I did hear some general concern about the continued decline of five-year, five-year-ahead inflation compensation and the risk it signals that longer-run inflation expectations could be starting to drift.	13	1
201501	3247	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	Of course, as many of you noted, statistical models we look at suggest that the downdrift mainly reflects a decline in the inflation risk premiums or liquidity premiums.	13	1
201501	3248	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	But as a number of you noted, it is not obvious, for reasons that Presidents Kocherlakota and Evans discussed, that this is an entirely comforting interpretation.	13	1
201501	3249	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	And I also noted comments that, while survey measures may be stable, this is not entirely reassuring, as we saw stability of longer-run inflation expectations in Japan as well.	13	1
201501	3250	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	So, clearly, this is something that needs close monitoring.	13	1

201501	3255	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	Then I’d like to add just a couple of comments of my own.	13	1
201501	3256	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	I certainly agree with everyone that incoming data provide confirmation that the labor market is making good progress toward a more normal state.	13	1
201501	3257	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	And, knock on wood, I am optimistic that we will see continued progress toward fulfilling the employment leg of our dual mandate.	13	1

201501	3260	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	In addition, when measured on a cyclically adjusted basis, the number of long-term unemployed workers and the percentage of involuntary part-time workers remains elevated, while the labor force participation rate remains low.	13	1

201501	3264	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	In contrast, incoming data—particularly the lower-than-expected core CPI inflation reading for December, the continued decline in oil prices, and continued upward pressure on the dollar—have, to my mind, made the outlook for inflation somewhat less satisfactory.	13	1

201501	3270	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	The stability of survey measures continues to reassure me that longer-run inflation expectations remain reasonably well anchored, but I, too, am increasingly disturbed by the marked decline we have seen in far-dated measures of inflation compensation in recent months.	13	1
201501	3271	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	I also would note that financial market data indicate an increased desire on the part of investors to insure against very low inflation, and that worries me—that there is increased concern on the part of investors that the economy may fall into a state of very low inflation or even deflation in some regions.	13	1
201501	3272	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	Like many of you, I, too, am perplexed by the remarkably low level of long-term interest rates, which I find hard to square with our December SEP assessments of the appropriate path of the federal funds rate.	13	1
201501	3273	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	One possible explanation is that while we and the market have similar views of the modal path for domestic short-term interest rates, investors perceive a distribution of possible outcomes that places significant probability on low interest rate scenarios.	13	1
201501	3274	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	For example, investors may see a high risk that poor economic performance abroad will result in a persistent glut of foreign savings, resulting in a chronically strong dollar, a growing US current account deficit, a low equilibrium real funds rate, and subdued inflation.	13	1
201501	3275	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	If investors prove to be right, we’re apt to boost the funds rate much less than we envision in the SEP forecasts, but in this low r* world, the zero lower bound and “reach-for-yield” investor behavior will continue to be policy concerns for us for the foreseeable future.	13	1
201501	3276	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	On the other hand, investors may be too pessimistic about global risks, and they may alter their views over time.	13	1
201501	3277	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	And if that’s the case, I worry we could be in for quite a bumpy ride.	13	1
201501	3278	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	Increased investor optimism about the global outlook could prompt a significant fall in the dollar and rise in longer-term interest rates.	13	1
201501	3279	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	If such adjustments occur quickly, we could see significant disruptions in financial markets.	13	1
201501	3280	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	Finally, it could be that the very low level of long-term interest rates simply reflects a decline in term premiums abroad in response to foreign monetary policy easing that, in turn, has led to lower longer-term yields domestically as market participants worldwide apparently reach for higher-yielding safe investments without overly worrying about exchange rate risk.	13	1
201501	3281	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	The same mechanism may also be a factor boosting equity prices.	13	1
201501	3282	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	In this case, absent a steeper path for policy rates than we anticipate, financial conditions may remain more accommodative than we intend, although the strength of the dollar provides at least a partial offset.	13	1
201501	3283	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	The persistence of such a conundrum will also likely have policy consequences.	13	1
201501	3284	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	These scenarios hardly exhaust the set of possible explanations for the current constellation of financial market prices, but I think they highlight some of the risks that are present in the current situation.	13	1

201501	3300	2015-01-28	meeting	FROST	In yesterday’s discussion, the Committee expressed interest in having the staff put forward two proposals for testing term RRPs—one that includes further testing of term RRPs over the March quarter- end only and one that includes the March quarter-end tests and a series of small-term RRP tests in February and early March.	92	9
201501	3301	2015-01-28	meeting	FROST	The first option—further testing of term RRPs over the March quarter-end— would include the four operational refinements that Lorie mentioned yesterday.	92	9
201501	3302	2015-01-28	meeting	FROST	These refinements are based on lessons learned from the tests over year-end and are outlined in the top panel of exhibit 1.	92	9
201501	3303	2015-01-28	meeting	FROST	To reiterate, the four refinements are:  one, reduce the amount of term RRP offered to $200 billion to improve our understanding of the effect of decreased capacity; two, have the term RRPs mature across multiple days in early April to avoid operational risks associated with having all of the operations mature on the same day; three, make modest changes to the frequency and tenor of term RRP operations, resulting in fewer operations and shorter maturities; and four, express the maximum bid rate as a spread above the ON RRP rate on the day of the operation to better allow for the possibility of a change in the target for the federal funds rate following the announcement of the term RRP offerings but before the actual operations commence.	92	9
201501	3304	2015-01-28	meeting	FROST	The second option includes a series of small tests of term RRPs in February and early March, in addition to the March quarter-end tests that I just discussed.	92	9
201501	3305	2015-01-28	meeting	FROST	A possible structure of these non-quarter-end tests is outlined in the bottom panel of exhibit 1.	92	9
201501	3306	2015-01-28	meeting	FROST	If the Committee wanted to pursue these tests, it could authorize the Desk to offer a series of relatively short-term RRPs in modest sizes to learn more about the degree of substitutability between overnight and term RRPs, and to get more insight into the rate the market requires on term RRPs to induce such substitution.	92	9
201501	3307	2015-01-28	meeting	FROST	To best balance the desire to learn about these issues with an inclination to keep the size of such tests relatively small, the Committee might want to instruct the staff to conduct a series of term RRP operations in February and March with a size limit of $50 billion outstanding at any one time.	92	9
201501	3308	2015-01-28	meeting	FROST	As is the case with the quarter-end term RRP testing, this limit would be in addition to the $300 billion daily limit on the overnight RRP.	92	9
201501	3309	2015-01-28	meeting	FROST	These operations could be conducted beginning on Thursday, February 12, and all mature by Thursday, March 12.	92	9
201501	3310	2015-01-28	meeting	FROST	These dates would ensure that these operations do not conflict with the March quarter-end term RRP testing, and all of these operations would all mature prior to the Committee’s March meeting.	92	9
201501	3311	2015-01-28	meeting	FROST	The operations could also be subject to a maximum bid rate of the prevailing ON RRP rate plus 5 basis points, and be awarded via a single-price auction.	92	9
201501	3312	2015-01-28	meeting	FROST	While the exact terms of these tests would be determined in the coming days, it is likely that we would plan to start relatively small at around $10 billion and utilize a maximum bid rate of the prevailing ON RRP rate plus 5 basis points, and conduct a series of four one-week operations every Thursday from February 12 through March 5.	92	9
201501	3313	2015-01-28	meeting	FROST	In the coming days, Simon Potter would inform the Committee of the detailed terms of the first of the planned operations after obtaining the Chair’s approval.	92	9
201501	3314	2015-01-28	meeting	FROST	Depending on which option the Committee chooses, the Desk could release a statement following this meeting, with more operational details announced through a future Desk statement.	92	9
201501	3315	2015-01-28	meeting	FROST	Draft resolutions authorizing each type of term RRP testing are provided in your handout as exhibit 2.	92	9
201501	3316	2015-01-28	meeting	FROST	We will have copies of the Desk statement associated with each of the options in case you would like to review.	92	9

201501	3463	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	I’d like to call on Ed Nelson to get us started off—to introduce this topic.	21	2
201501	3464	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	Thank you, Madam Chair.	21	2
201501	3465	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	I will be referring to the handout labeled “Material for Briefing on the Timing of Liftoff and the Pace of Tightening.”	21	2
201501	3466	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	Earlier this month, the staff distributed a packet of five memos that detailed how considerations related to monetary policy strategy might figure into policymakers’ decisions about the timing and pace of policy firming.	21	2
201501	3467	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	My remarks will distill some key messages from this material.	21	2
201501	3468	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	The first memo, by Mike Kiley, Dave Reifschneider, and myself, discusses various considerations that could influence your decisions regarding the timing and pace of adjustments to the policy stance in the period ahead.	21	2
201501	3469	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	As you know from Tealbook B, many simple interest rate rules, as well as optimal-control policy, call for an immediate policy firming.	21	2
201501	3470	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	However, research suggests that the overall trajectory of the federal funds rate expected by market participants is much more important for the behavior of real activity and inflation than is the precise choice of date at which to commence policy firming.	21	2
201501	3471	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	As the memo noted, alternative benchmark policies that incorporate inertia, such as the inertial Taylor (1999) rule and the optimal-control policy, imply a smaller initial tightening and a more gradual subsequent pace of tightening than do the noninertial Taylor (1993 and 1999) rules.	21	2
201501	3472	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	In simulations of the FRB/US model conditioned on the staff forecast, this gradual pace facilitates a more rapid return of inflation toward the 2 percent objective, in part because the policy allows the unemployment rate to run below the natural rate of unemployment for a time.	21	2
201501	3473	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	Another point highlighted in the memo is that your December SEP paths for the federal funds rate differ appreciably from the path implied by the noninertial Taylor (1999) rule.	21	2
201501	3474	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	These deviations, which diminish notably over the projection period, may suggest that you have a preference for interest rate smoothing.	21	2
201501	3475	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	Alternatively, they may indicate that your views concerning appropriate policy are influenced by factors that incline you to take a cautious approach to normalizing the stance of monetary policy.	21	2
201501	3476	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	For example, some of you may worry that the economy remains somewhat fragile, on the grounds that the pickup in economic growth is so recent, or you may fear that an adverse shock could prompt serious retrenchments in spending and hiring, with attendant financial instability.	21	2
201501	3477	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	Containing these risks might be a particular concern for the Committee because the effective lower bound on policy rates and the already-elevated size of the balance sheet could limit the Federal Reserve’s flexibility in responding to negative shocks.	21	2
201501	3478	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	In addition, some of you may see potential advantages to allowing the unemployment rate to undershoot estimates of the natural rate for a time; these advantages might include speeding the return to 2 percent inflation, or the opportunity to explore whether some portion of the recent supply-side damage can be reversed.	21	2
201501	3479	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	As the memo also notes, however, adopting a cautious approach to tightening could be costly if taken too far, because it could generate an undesirably large increase in inflation or undermine financial stability.	21	2
201501	3480	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	Another memo to the Committee, from de Groot, Gagnon, and Tetlow, considers evidence from stochastic simulations of the FRB/US model of two alternative policy strategies:  an “earlier and gradual” firming strategy and a “later and steep” strategy.	21	2
201501	3481	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	The authors find that—provided that the data-dependent nature of Federal Reserve policy is well understood by the private sector—the choice between the two strategies is not of crucial importance, as measured by a policymaker loss function, under most scenarios.	21	2
201501	3482	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	Each strategy is associated with prospective costs: An earlier-and-gradual strategy entails a higher probability of returning to the effective lower bound, while a later-and-steep strategy raises the probability that policymakers might have to raise the federal funds rate very rapidly.	21	2
201501	3483	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	Two staff memos discuss what can be learned from prior tightening episodes.	21	2
201501	3484	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	One of these memos, by Meade, Nozawa, Petrasek, and Zickler, discusses FOMC deliberations that occurred around the initial tightening of monetary policy in 1994, 1999, and 2004.	21	2
201501	3485	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	It shows that, in all cases, the decisions to tighten monetary policy were data dependent and forward looking.	21	2
201501	3486	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	Expansion of the Federal Reserve’s communications tools likely left financial markets better prepared for policy firming in 1999 and 2004 than they were in 1994, when the market reaction to the initial tightening was sizable.	21	2
201501	3487	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	The second of these memos, by De Michelis, De Pooter, and Wood, reviews the international experience with raising policy rates from the effective lower bound.	21	2
201501	3488	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	Central banks differed in the precise manner in which they signaled their intention to transition from the effective-lower-bound policy; however, a common feature was that they provided broad guidance about the time frame or economic conditions in which policy firming would commence.	21	2
201501	3489	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	In all cases, regardless of communications strategy, policy firming did not prove disruptive to financial markets.	21	2
201501	3490	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	In addition, no case was found in which policy firming was too late or otherwise insufficient.	21	2
201501	3491	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	Among the episodes considered, Japan in 2000 and Sweden in 2010 provide possible instances in which the tightening was premature or excessive, perhaps because policymakers gave undue weight to factors other than inflation and economic activity when making their decisions to raise rates.	21	2
201501	3492	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	In both of these instances, policy firming was subsequently reversed and the policy rate returned to the effective lower bound.	21	2
201501	3493	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	Canada, in contrast, had a more orderly experience when it tightened policy starting in 2010, perhaps because the firming was gradual and was closely keyed to macroeconomic conditions.	21	2
201501	3494	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	Focusing specifically on communications, the memo by English, Laubach, and Reeve offers language—reproduced on the third and fourth pages of your handout— that the Committee might consider employing in policy statements as it approaches and reaches the time at which policy firming is commenced.	21	2
201501	3495	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	In addition, the final section of my memo with Kiley and Reifschneider noted two points that the Committee might wish to highlight in speeches, testimony, and other forums: One:  Once firming commences, the federal funds rate will not follow a predetermined trajectory; instead, all adjustments will be data dependent.	21	2
201501	3496	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	Two:  Real activity could turn out to be stronger and inflation higher than expected, implying that policy would need to tighten more rapidly than envisioned in the SEP paths.	21	2
201501	3497	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	Conversely, conditions could turn out to be weaker, making it appropriate to tighten more slowly or even to reverse course.	21	2
201501	3498	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	Furthermore, policymakers might come to a consensus that special considerations—for example, a desire to lower the likelihood that the Committee would need to return to the effective lower bound—were playing an important role in its deliberations.	21	2
201501	3499	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	If so, the Committee might wish to describe these considerations in its policy communications.	21	2
201501	3500	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	Along with the memos, the staff distributed a few questions that you may wish to address in your comments during today’s go-round.	21	2
201501	3501	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	For your convenience, these questions are repeated on the last page of your handout.	21	2
201501	3502	2015-01-28	meeting	NELSON	Thank you, and I would be happy to take any questions you might have.	21	2

201501	3681	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	Thank you, Madam Chair.	22	2
201501	3682	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	I expect conditions will make liftoff in 2015 appropriate.	22	2
201501	3683	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	But, in that context, I have a strong preference for erring on the side of waiting a little longer rather than taking the risks of moving prematurely.	22	2
201501	3684	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	I am assuming that the sequence of actions will be a signaling decision two, possibly one, meeting ahead of liftoff involving a change of statement language, an intervening meeting statement in the case of two meetings ahead that reinforces the high likelihood of a liftoff decision at the next meeting, and then the decision.	22	2
201501	3685	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	I’m comfortable with this approach.	22	2
201501	3686	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	I do not have great confidence that the Committee can preserve a lot of flexibility once the signaling decision has been made and communicated, unless there is a development that is obvious justification for pushing the pause button.	22	2
201501	3687	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	I view liftoff as close to a fait accompli once the signal is given.	22	2
201501	3688	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	Therefore, from a timing standpoint, I think the right question is how to weigh the risks of signaling liftoff prematurely versus the risks of waiting.	22	2
201501	3689	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	I think an earlier signal would entail significant risks if the context of that decision is one of little or no evidence of inflation moving back to target, accompanied by a mixed data picture and higher-than-normal uncertainty.	22	2
201501	3690	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	I would also adjust the second question to, what developments do I need to see in the data in order to be willing to signal that a liftoff decision is coming in, say, 90 days?	22	2
201501	3691	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	I am prepared to take the first liftoff step in the absence of clearly affirmative evidence on inflation, as long as there is not a troubling accumulation of contrary evidence.	22	2
201501	3692	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	While that is a minimalist take on data dependency, I am comfortable with this approach, in light of the current apparent strength of the real economy.	22	2
201501	3693	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	But I can imagine a framework of affirmative evidence—call this my ideal framework.	22	2
201501	3694	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	Ideally, I’d like to see core inflation readings moving above 1½ percent.	22	2
201501	3695	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	I don’t think a “run rate” of core inflation below 1½ percent is confidence-inspiring.	22	2
201501	3696	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	Ideally, I’d like to see nominal wage growth increasing according to most measures of labor compensation.	22	2
201501	3697	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	And, ideally, I’d like to see clearly anchored inflation expectations.	22	2
201501	3698	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	I would characterize those criteria as affirmative evidence.	22	2
201501	3699	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	As I said, I won’t hold out for that degree of data dependency.	22	2
201501	3700	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	At a minimum, I’d like to see evidence that the transitory effect of energy-related prices is dissipating.	22	2
201501	3701	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	I’m concerned that it would be bad “optics” to signal rate increases in the midst of a string of headline inflation reports that are moving away from our longer-term objective.	22	2
201501	3702	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	I’d like to see evidence of a reversal of the recent disinflation trend seen in core inflation and less ambiguous readings on inflation expectations.	22	2
201501	3703	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	Now, I can imagine real-economy developments that might substantially mitigate my concerns about incoming inflation data.	22	2
201501	3704	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	Data that indicate significant overshoot of my baseline expectations for GDP and jobs growth would bolster my confidence that inflation is very likely to move in the desired direction.	22	2

201501	3708	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	Even if in casual usage the expression can mean an indefinite small number, I think it will be taken to mean two meetings.	22	2

201501	3715	2015-01-28	meeting	LOCKHART	Concerns about the combination of high unemployment and high inflation have diminished, but that concern has been replaced, in my thinking, with a significant concern about a combination of unemployment below its full-employment rate and inflation well below target.	22	2

201501	3719	2015-01-28	meeting	YELLEN	President Rosengren.	22	2
201501	3720	2015-01-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	Thank you, Madam Chair.	22	2
201501	3721	2015-01-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	My comments are quite consistent with President Lockhart’s.	22	2
201501	3722	2015-01-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	I’m going to answer each of the three questions that was posed to us.	22	2
201501	3723	2015-01-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	For question 1, the risks at liftoff remain asymmetric.	22	2
201501	3724	2015-01-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	We have the ability to raise short- term rates more quickly, but we do not have the ability to reduce short-term rates below the zero bound.	22	2
201501	3725	2015-01-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	Thus, as long as we are limited in our ability to offset negative shocks, and given my view that the risks of negative shocks from abroad are elevated, I would rather wait longer and, if needed, tighten faster should we have underestimated how quickly we would reach both elements of our dual mandate.	22	2
201501	3726	2015-01-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	The global disinflation in developed economies adds further to the risks of significantly undershooting our inflation goal—the one goal for which we set an explicit target.	22	2
201501	3727	2015-01-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	In terms of question 2, I agree with the Chair’s assessment that, before we lift off, we need to be reasonably confident that inflation will be moving up over time.	22	2
201501	3728	2015-01-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	Being reasonably confident would require that we have tangible evidence that wages and/or prices are moving in a way to ensure reaching our 2 percent goal for inflation within a reasonable horizon.	22	2
201501	3729	2015-01-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	Thus, I would be careful not to rely too much on forecasts based on models that have been consistently overly optimistic about our return to our inflation goal, nor am I comforted that inflation surveys are not falling when a wide variety of market indicators are signaling disinflationary concerns and have been doing so since October despite tightening labor markets.	22	2
201501	3730	2015-01-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	I would be uncomfortable removing accommodation before we see core inflation rising rather than falling, or strong evidence in disaggregated wage and price data that tighter labor markets are starting to move wages and prices in a way consistent with reaching our inflation goal within a two-year time frame.	22	2
201501	3731	2015-01-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	In terms of question 3, given the uncertainty about both global markets and the uncertainty surrounding our inflation forecast, I would not constrain the Committee to provide a two-meeting warning prior to our liftoff.	22	2
201501	3732	2015-01-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	We have already telegraphed that liftoff is quite possible this year.	22	2
201501	3733	2015-01-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	My preference would be to drop the “patient” language the meeting before we expect to liftoff, and to say, “An increase in the federal funds target will likely be warranted soon, if economic conditions continue to improve.”	22	2
201501	3734	2015-01-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	I agree with President Lockhart that we should not try to overcalibrate our communication strategy.	22	2
201501	3735	2015-01-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	Thank you, Madam Chair.	22	2

201501	3738	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	Thank you.	22	2
201501	3739	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	I guess, as Monty Python would say, “And now for something completely different.”	22	2
201501	3740	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	[Laughter]	22	2
201501	3741	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	I’m going to address each of the questions posed to the Committee.	22	2
201501	3742	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	Regarding how the FOMC should weigh the risk of lifting off too soon versus the risk of waiting too long, with economic growth strengthening, full employment approaching, inflation expected to move gradually back to target, and our policy rate at zero, I believe we may be underestimating the costs of moving too slowly.	22	2
201501	3743	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	The analysis in one of the staff memos that uses the FRB/US model indicates that there are similar macroeconomic outcomes associated with following either an early gradual policy path or a later steep path.	22	2
201501	3744	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	I’m a firm proponent of using models to inform our policy decisions.	22	2
201501	3745	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	But while our typical models may give us a reasonably accurate sense of the costs of lifting off too soon, they may not accurately reflect all the costs of waiting too long.	22	2
201501	3746	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	If we lift off too soon, we’ll encounter employment and inflation costs that standard models reasonably capture.	22	2
201501	3747	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	Although the zero lower bound may exacerbate these costs, our models are sophisticated enough to provide some help in judging these costs.	22	2
201501	3748	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	If we wait too long to lift off, there may be benefits from avoiding future episodes of the zero lower bound—benefits that the model captures.	22	2
201501	3749	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	But there are other potential costs, including the risks to financial stability of holding rates at zero for a long time.	22	2
201501	3750	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	Our models aren’t sophisticated enough to allow us to calibrate those costs and risks, yet we know from the crisis that the costs can be severe.	22	2
201501	3751	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	In addition, there may be value in initially moving rates up gradually.	22	2
201501	3752	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	Although we believe we have the tools, we have not yet raised rates from the zero lower bound with a large balance sheet.	22	2
201501	3753	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	I would like to gain some experience when we are in a position to raise rates gradually rather than when we feel compelled to move rates up quickly.	22	2
201501	3754	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	Although the Committee has been appropriately cautious up to now, I’m less concerned, in view of the strength of the economy, that the start of liftoff will negatively affect the expansion.	22	2
201501	3755	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	Monetary policy will still be highly accommodative after the first rate increase.	22	2
201501	3756	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	That’s not to say that after we start raising rates we will necessarily continue on a predetermined path up.	22	2
201501	3757	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	The future is always uncertain; negative shocks can hit the economy at any time.	22	2
201501	3758	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	If incoming economic information materially changes our outlook, we would change our policy.	22	2
201501	3759	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	We may want to slow down the path of rate increases; we may want to reverse course.	22	2
201501	3760	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	But that’s what a data-dependent central banker is supposed to do.	22	2
201501	3761	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	I don’t see that as a problem.	22	2
201501	3762	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	Regarding what developments I would need to see to be more or less confident that inflation would move back to 2 percent over time, first, economic research shows that it is very difficult to forecast inflation.	22	2
201501	3763	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	Even in normal times, our inflation forecasts aren’t very precise.	22	2
201501	3764	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	The confidence bands around the forecast are wide.	22	2
201501	3765	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	Under today’s unusual circumstances, I don’t think it’s appropriate to require a higher-than-normal degree of precision regarding the forecast.	22	2
201501	3766	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	I am reasonably comfortable with the forecast that inflation will gradually move back up to 2 percent.	22	2
201501	3767	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	Continued strong economic growth, and gains in the labor market, and longer- term inflation expectations remaining stable will buoy confidence in that forecast.	22	2
201501	3768	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	Historically, economic recoveries have been associated with gradual increases in underlying inflation.	22	2
201501	3769	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	Should economic growth falter or survey measures of inflation expectations begin to trend downward, I’d be less confident in the forecast.	22	2
201501	3770	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	I want to note that I do not need to see wages accelerate to convince me that inflation will rise.	22	2
201501	3771	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	Federal Reserve Bank of Cleveland staff analysis that examined several measures of wages and broader labor compensation found that the lead-lag relationship between nominal wage growth and price inflation is tenuous.	22	2
201501	3772	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	The strongest correlations are contemporaneous ones, especially since the mid-1980s, and my staff found no evidence that wages and labor compensation help to predict price inflation out of sample.	22	2
201501	3773	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	Finally, regarding statement language, I do think the suggestions in the staff memo for how the language might evolve in advance of liftoff provide a useful approach.	22	2
201501	3774	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	However, as I indicated in the memo I circulated to the FOMC last week, I believe we could take the opportunity to simplify the statement’s organization in an effective way.	22	2
201501	3775	2015-01-28	meeting	MESTER	Thank you, Madam Chair.	22	2
